		<shortDescription>I know it's a classic, but try as I might, I can't get into it. Here's why.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've seen Notorious several times. It is considered one of Hitchcock's great movies, and it certainly contains some skillfully created suspense scenes (the champagne in the ice bucket is kind of brilliant). Yet, I have never particularly enjoyed the film, and have only rewatched it in hopes of figuring out why people like it. But this last time, I got a clearer idea of why I don't.First off, there's the cast. Claude Rains is great, but I'm not so hot for Grant and Bergman. Bergman is simply totally unconvincing as a party girl. She is a girl you cast as a nun or a secretary, not as a hard- drinking girl who likes to sleep around.And while I usually find Grant very likable, in this movie he is anything but. He is an out and out jerk. Yes, I understand a lot of the conflict is between her need for words and his for actions, but it doesn't make him less of a jerk, nor make her anything other than an idiot.The story itself has many issues. First off, the whole idea is a bit scatterbrained, and the fact that Bergman isn't told her assignment until she's done there seems pretty ridiculous; nine out of ten people would take the next flight home. This movie is one where Hitchcok's McGuffin concept fails. For those not familiar with the anecdote, the McGuffin refers to that thing everyone in the movie and no one in the audience cares about.It's a clever conceit, but it only works if you care about what happens to the people in the movie. It didn't matter what was going on in North By Northwest, but we were rooting for Grant to survive. There is simply nothing I care about in Notorious. It is just two miscast actors chasing after something no one is even supposed to care about. If you want to see Hitchcock the technician, it's a good movie to watch, because he does a lot of nice things in it. But for me, it's just a difficult movie to love.</fullReview>

		<username>johnnyboyz</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Great study of certain character relationships on top of an interesting espionage story.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hitchcock's early work is usually very interesting to go back to and view. Notorious is no exception in that it demonstrates Hitchcock's ability to set up a situation and get across a great deal of suspense and angst through very simple techniques. If art cinema thrives on emphasising the ordinary or seemingly unnecessary and making it come across as quite broad and epic then Notorious succeeds at exactly this at certain times, specifically when champagne and champagne bottles are involved as tension and foreboding is racked up.Made during America's 'Golden Age'; the classical Hollywood era, Notorious delivers an interesting story second to an equally intriguing character focus revolving around T.R. Devlin (Grant) and Alicia Huberman (Bergman), one an agent and one wrapped into helping said agent and his organisation in tracking down some Nazis whom have escaped to South America. This springs forward two things that come to immediate attention. The first is the year in which the film was made and released that being 1946 and the historical significance in the sense World War Two had just ended ? the other being the move by the studio to have an American character and a German one get so involved with one another shortly after such a war took place.But like I said, the concentration of this film is the chemistry between the two lead characters and the dialogue and exchanges they are involved in. The genius here is that everything is established between the two individuals in such a quick and effective manner that we don't need long and complicated build ups to these leads. Similarly to Double Indemnity from two years prior, the two are introduced and the script just takes over. The dialogue is snappy and sharp, the delivery cynical and humorous and the instant feelings we evoke towards the exchanges plant a seed within our sub-conscious that sticks throughout the duration of the film whenever these two are on screen together. Again similarly to Double Indemnity we get the feeling these two together, working together, will provide us, the audience, with dramatic or explosive results and the film does not disappoint.Part of the reason the relationship works so well, even today, is the sense and feeling of the characters. We feel as if Alicia has seen her fair share of situations and whatever ammunition Devlin has for her can be easily dealt with. Whatever position or situation she might face, we get the immense notion she's up to it and this combined with the fact she's German at a time of great unrest towards said nation, must've totalled up to an enthralling viewing back in the day. But the plot for the film introduces a little more to read into as far as supporting and antagonistic characters go. If the primary focus of relationships is on Devlin and Alicia then the secondary focus is certainly on the relationship between Alexander Sebastian (Rains) and his mother, Anna Sebastian (Konstantin), in all its disturbing glory. As a twosome, Anna acts as the superego over her son and attempts to dominate him in a mother/son partnership not experimented with by Hitchcock until he did so on a much greater scale in 1960's Psycho.This mother/son antagonism acts as a sort of pit-stop for the audience away from Devlin and Alicia's relationship which, as I said, begins cynical and oppositional but blossoms into something much more; something Devlin himself finds very painful to take when the scenes involving Alicia and Alex play out during which Devlin is a witness. The eerie thing here is that while Alex's lust for Alicia could be read into to ring true, it is Devlin that Alicia really loves and thus, any embrace with Alex is false. Throw into the mix the earlier scenes of motherly domination and you have a cocktail of love, lust, embrace and Freudian theory all cooking on a slow burner simultaneously.But Hitchcock's skill in unfolding a relationship whatever the nature is not his limit and this film demonstrates it perfectly. His use of suspense through an inanimate object and basic editing is something you could learn from, particularly through method of set up. At one point, a character must gain access to a key on a chain that is difficult to remove but that is only in order to move onto the next suspense set piece: a trip down into the depths of a large house to find something else. Questions arise of a specific nature: 'will this character succeed in getting the key?'; 'will the other character notice said object is missing?' and once they're down in the basement, the constant cutting to a from the champagne bottles running out acts as a wonderful cut away. As an actress playing a character, Ingrid Bergman plays Alicia with a stone faced efficiency. She is betraying people of her own nation and has an expression that pains her to come to terms with who and what she is: a seductress. But the fact her character is so good at what she does only adds an extra layer of disturbance. Notorious was, I suppose, quite daring for its time given the relationship it had going on between an American and a German character a mother and her son but if anything, it still acts as a fascinating espionage tale.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>HelloTexas11</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>one of the best endings ever</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'Notorious' is the latest discovery in my ongoing Alfred Hitchcock Film Festival. Never seen it before, had no idea what it was about. First impressions- Ingrid Bergman acts circles around Cary Grant, whom I have never seen in a more unappealing role. Don't get me wrong, he's very good too, as is Claude Rains... but this is Bergman's show, maybe the best performance she ever gave, which is saying something. Made and released in 1946, 'Notorious' may have been the first mainstream film to feature a 'Nazis after the war' plot. God knows there have been hundreds since; as critic Joe Adamson succinctly put it, "the Nazis, though recently defeated, were embarking on a long cinematic career that will probably stretch well into the next century." Sure enough, here they are scheming in South America, a small group of them, living well off plundered loot no doubt, adhering to a strict code of secrecy and trusting no one, not even each other. It is a very paranoid clan, that paranoia embodied by Madame Sebastian, the old but crafty and domineering mother of Alex Sebastion (Claude Rains), first among equals of the Nazi group. (Hitchcock does love his domineering mothers.) Alicia Huberman (Bergman) is recruited to infiltrate the ring and find out what they're up to; her father has just been convicted of being a Nazi spy, and so she has connections with the group and Alex Sebastian in particular, who has something of a crush on her. Alicia, however, is a patriotic American, something the Nazis are unaware of; they just assume her loyalty was to her father. Devlin (Cary Grant) is the government agent who recruits her and acts as her contact during her mission. I suppose if I have a problem with 'Notorious,' it's the relationship between Devlin and Alicia. Apropos of nothing really, they strike up a romance, though you'd be hard-pressed to call it that judging from Devlin's behavior. He is constantly casting aspersions on her character with crude and spiteful remarks, acting as though he holds her in complete contempt most of the time. The suggestion is that she's a drunk and worse than that, a prostitute for agreeing to the very mission he recruits her for! This misogynistic attitude is shared more or less by the other intelligence officials to whom Alicia reports, including Prescott (Louis Calhern) and frankly, it bothers me. It is compounded by the fact that Alicia is in love with Devlin, again, for no apparent good reason. Is it because he looks down on her? The fact that Devlin's true feelings are made known to her in the last five minutes does not make up for the way he treats her throughout the rest of the movie. I know times were different then and I generally have no problem making allowances for past attitudes, but this strikes me as a very unattractive male fantasy of sorts, one that was very common in the forties and fifties. But that aside, 'Notorious' is most intriguing and full of tension in that unique Hitchcock style. Alicia is finally found out by Alex, but by this point they have been married and he realizes he cannot expose her without coming under suspicion from his fellow Nazis himself. So he and his mother hit upon the idea of slowly poisoning her, over the course of several weeks. She misses a meeting with Devlin and so he goes to Sebastian's home to check up on her, where he finds out from her what has happened. Alicia is very weak as Devlin leads her down the stairs; they are met by Alex and Madame Sebastian as the other Nazis watch from below. Alex must watch helplessly as Devlin and Alicia walk slowly from his house to his car; Alex follows and whispers desperately for them to take him, too, but Devlin refuses. The final shot shows the Nazis asking Alex to come back inside; they have a few questions. An absolutely brilliant ending.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2005-03-15</date>
		<username>Ralphie Giordano</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A "Notorious" Classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When you think of an Alfred Hitchcock movie, it is obvious that you would think of the word classic. Notorious, his riveting 1947 drama is no exception. Notorious is an extremely well written fast paced drama with superb directing and outstanding acting. The story centers around Alicia (played by Ingrid Bergman) and Devlin (Cary Grant). After her Nazi father is convicted to twenty years in prison for treason, Alicia is pursued by Devlin, an American secret agent. Devlin wants Alicia to go undercover and infiltrate her father's Nazi friend's home for information. The eventual love that Alicia and Devlin find for each other leads to Alicia's decision to agree to this assignment. Alicia expresses her loyalty to her country, and agrees to go to South America and forge love to her father's friend. In order to succeed, Alicia must be willing to sacrifice her freedom and possibly herself. Devlin must put his love for her aside and allow her to do so. What follows in the storyline revolves around the question, how far will both Alicia and Devlin go in order for the mission to be a success?Despite the fact that Notorious' writer, Ben Hecht, was nominated for a 1947 Academy Award for best original screenplay, I feel that majority of the story is told through the movement and placement of the camera. The camera portrayed how the characters were feeling, without them having to say it. The actors themselves should also be credited for performing so well, but most of the acclaim should go to the cinematography and direction of the film. Hitchcock's choreography of the camera was a work of art. Frequently during the movie, while Alicia and Devlin were conversing, the camera is strictly on them, and unfocused on the scenery around them. This is done purposely by Hitchcock, to portray that regardless of what is going on in the two characters surroundings, they are intensely focused only on each other. The fact that this is revealed without words and through the camera is one of the finest aspects of the film.At the time of making the film, the Production Code was in full effect. Many directors and writers were limited on what they were permissible to show on screen. Hitchcock, however, did not take the production code as a hindrance to his work. Instead, he worked around the code to make the film just as well, if not superior than it would be without the code. The best example of this is what Hitchcock did concerning the three-second kiss rule. The script had a scene involving a long passionate kiss involving the two main characters of the story. However, the production code stated that no kiss can be longer then three seconds long, yet the scene wound be being referred by many as "the longest kiss scene in film history." The "longest" kiss was actually a serious of short kisses with passionate dialogue in between each kiss. According to Roger Ebert of the Chicago Sun-Times, the three-second rule led to a much better scene. I could not agree more. This shows how much talent Hitchcock had as a director. He was prohibited to make the kiss long, and he transformed it into a masterpiece of a scene. All throughout the movie, Hitchcock used ways to get around the production code, and still tell the story the best he can.After viewing Alfred Hitchcock's Notorious, you will be left breathless. However, all of the credit should not be only directed to Hitchcock. It is a combination of amazing acting, writing and directing that makes this such a classic. Notorious is a definite recommendation for anyone who enjoys cinema.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>One of the few perfect Hollywood films!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Alfred Hitchcock was hitting on all eight cylinders when he directed this classic film. Every scene seems to be shot with care, not only with concerns of camera placement/movement, but also acting. Cary Grant is surprisingly somber, even hateful, in one of his most un-Grant-ish roles. Ingrid Bergman is simply magnificent, far better than even her great role in "Casablanca" and she rarely ever achieved this level of perfection again. Claude Rains...the third lead in the film...is one of the bad guys, yes, but he is also kind, polite, considerate, caring. A real gentleman.This last point is worth pointing out because Hitchcock always seemed to be interested in showing us not only villains, but also heroes, who go against type. Think of "Secret Agent", with Robert Young playing a very nice guy, but wait....he turns out to be the villain! Or, 1971's "Frenzy", Barry Foster is a wonderful guy, a friend to all, certainly a more likable guy than the boorish hero. Now take a look at Claude Rains. In an era when Nazi's were portrayed almost to the point of wearing a handlebar mustache and a black cape, Rains (with Hitchcock's direction) presents us with a true gentleman who just happens to be a Nazi. Several scenes in this movie have been singled out over the years as a sign of Hitchcock's genius: the two-minute kissing scene; the revelation of the key; the discovery of the bottle; the poisoning of Bergman's character; the final sequence where Grant finally rescues Bergman. I have a personal favorite scene: the scene, on the plane, where Grant tells Bergman that her father has just committed suicide. Her speech (supposedly written by Hitchcock himself and inserted into the script) perfectly wraps up her character's complex emotional state. Simply filmed, wonderfully acted.One last note: the ending of this film is one of the most perfect, ever, for a Hollywood film. Once again pay attention to Claude Rains' facial expressions; they convey his fate as well as anything that could have been graphically filmed.**** out of four; 10/10; A+...all these ratings and grades describe this true classic!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2002-02-12</date>
		<username>davidholmesfr</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Up there with the best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's difficult to assert just what the keys are that make this a classic. Is it Hitchcock's direction or is it the acting of the three leads? Truth is, I guess, it's a combination. The script is tight and the story-line intriguing as the master builds the suspense, although, in truth, suspense plays second fiddle to the characters. Sebastian (Rains) is a Nazi sympathiser involved in a plot to develop atomic weaponry, presumably for a revivalist Nazi Germany of the future. Given this situation how easy it would have been to portray him as the personification of evil. But Hitchcock, by this time, was aware that black is not always black, any more than white is always white. So, Sebastian comes across, especially towards the end, as vulnerable and, if not deserving our support, at least we have a morsel of sympathy and understanding. Crossed in love by his `arranged' wife, Alicia (Bergman), and oppressed by his cigar-chewing dominant mother, he is no representative from the evil empire.At the same time Devlin (Grant), the `good' guy, treats Alicia cruelly, knowing that she loves him deeply. So whilst we should be with him we are so only reluctantly. Alicia comes over as the character most deserving of our sympathy, despite her loose living and love affair with the bottle. And what a performance from Bergman! The scene on the balcony over-looking Rio must be one of the outstanding love scenes in the history of cinema.With these characters and plot Hitchcock weaves a story of manipulative relationships based on love, desire, greed, politics, indeed the whole kit and caboodle. Unmissable cinema and well deserving of its high place in the all-time rankings.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Ingrid Bergman and that Ben Hecht script!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spoiler alert: this review is only for those who have seen "Notorious."The name Hitchcock needs to be mentioned first, of course, but really, it's Ingrid Bergman and Ben Hecht's script which make this rather dated film a delight. This is Ingrid Bergman's best performance, apologies to "Casablanca" fans, and Ben Hecht's screenplay is masterful.(He didn't just dip his pen in rattlesnake venom, his pen was immersed in MAMBA venom and the result is deliciously vicious).Hitchcock's camerawork is among his best and the swooping camera scene with the key is a feat of genius and deserves the praise which it has garnished. The wine cellar scene is as suspenseful as Hitchcock EVER got. He was a brilliant director, now on to what I really love about the film.Cary Grant was a passable dramatic actor. He is passable in this film, and in some scenes he's quite good. He wasn't miscast but his real strength--genius in my opinion--was comedy and lighthearted comedy dramas. His best comedies are his early ones but since this is a Hitchcock film, allow me one title: "North By Northwest." Need I say more?Ingrid Bergman is flat out superb in her best role (she deserved an Oscar) as the daughter-of-a-Nazi-who-makes-good-by-helping-Uncle-Sam and infiltrates Rains's ring in Rio. (Rains is marvelous, as always, in his role as a Mama's Boy who somehow heads an organization of much tougher Germans). In fact, I have more than once read that the relationship between Rains and his mother go to unholy extremes. That notion makes some sense, as this film was released less than a year after World War Two and Germans were then fair game.But from the opening scene to the closing one, it's Ingrid who bedazzles. She is completely believable in a somewhat unbelievable role, and the early scene where Grant puts a handkerchief around her bare midriff, "better wear this, you might catch cold," (as if in South Florida a hanky around the waist would make some kind of difference) is brief but very erotic and renders absurd the belief that "less is more" is untrue. Be it learning of the death of her father, her badinage with Grant, her wonderful English which she worked very hard to improve (and succeeded) her remorse, her liveliness; anything else one can imagine, this is Ingrid's film. Period. My favorite aspect of "Notorious:" Ben Hecht's script.Allow me a few quotes. Please remember the justifiable fury Hecht had for the Germans; the dialogue is still shocking and saturates the film. Just a few, beginning with the best and most famous one:"We are protected by the enormity of your stupidity." Mother to Rains, and THAT one is worthy of Bartlet's!"Look, I'll make it easy for you; the time has come when you must tell me that you have a wife and two adorable children and this madness between us can't go on." Ingrid. "I'll bet you've heard that line often enough." Grant. Ouch!"I'm terrified!" Ingrid in the wine cellar. "Pretend you're a janitor, janitors are never terrified." Grant."I'll have to be more careful, I turned my ankle the last time." The most brutal of the German gang saying (while wolfing a chicken leg) to the others that when he kills Emil via car, he'll need to jump farther than he previously had when he murdered by car an earlier person. Pure ice. And I love it! "Dry your eyes baby it's out of character." Grant to Bergman at the race track."I miss Emil. [Doctor Anderson, sounding contrite but then, after one second] He [Emil] was a first class metallurgist." Cuts to the bone.I love "Notorious" because of Hecht's script, and because Ingrid Bergman was never better. On a scale of ten, a 9.5.</fullReview>

Director Alfred Hitchcock mounts this espionage suspense-drama very carefully (some may say methodically), and the results are frosty and smooth (and occasionally quite enjoyable). But the film isn't high art, nor is it a genuine classic. It's pulp in fashionable dress. *** from ****</fullReview>

		<date>2001-03-14</date>
		<username>tedg (tedg@filmsfolded.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Not Effective Today</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spoilers herein.Hitchcock's work is as good as any to study for the impermanence of style. Let's completely set aside the clothes, the conceit about German spies/mothers-in-law, the obviously fake sets and the trite plot devices (uranium in bottles, poison in coffee). We are still left with two levels of style. I suppose it is arguable whether these are styles. I call them styles because they are imposed by society, were a major part of the original charm of the work, and now are noticeably out of place, replaced by a new set.The first concerns what it means to be cinematic: the staging, the framing and the relationship of lights to eye. Hitchcock was both a master and an innovator of the first two. But he was steeped in the theatrical tradition. Every actor showed that they knew where the camera was. The action comes to the camera, not the other way around. The perspective is constant, and at seated eye-level, seldom moving about as a disembodied observer might. This is obsolete today, even in the most mundane films. The second deals with modern acting. Hitchcock WAS modern sixty years ago. While he had one foot in the theater and an obligation to tell a story, he has the other foot in modern self-awareness. Incidentally marked by his walkons, his more pervasive use was how the actors winked at the camera. Grant was a master of this with his smile that was intended primarily for the viewer. So we have Grant in two persona: Devlin, and the actor playing Devlin. Now Hitchcock adds a familiar mix: Devlin and Alicia's characters become actors themselves. She to deceive the bad guys, he to deceive he.Actors playing actors is quite old (Shakespeare's favorite device) but to make it all self-referential comes from modern French comedy. Inserting the notion into `realistic' film was the Hitchcock innovation. But this style is passe today, as the sophistication of viewers has moved on. Now we have extremely multileveled, simultaneous roles even in our most low-brow films (`Nurse Betty'). Now we have the camera acting as a character. Now we have shifting narrative stances.All this shows the danger of being a stylish filmmaker -- you go out of style and your film just stops working. This film is of historical interest only -- it only works if you deliberately assume the mantle of the past -- which is enjoyment enough for some.Bergman IS lovely, and I think if she were acting today, she wouldn't be so stiff; and I presume this would detract from her appeal. Check out her daughter for instance in "Fearless."</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>"Notorious" is wickedly suspenseful and beautifully shot. Carey Grant is great as usual here but it's Ingrid Bergman who steals the picture. There is no better Alfred Hitchcock film that perfectly sums up his sensibility as a director. Only Hitchcock can deliver such fantastic camera movements and great suspense at the end of the movie. I knew that Hitchcock could build suspense, but I was unaware of his ability to tell a ravishing love story .Where the film shines is in the visual style, and the fact that while very few people in the film make any real mention of the love "affair" in the film, everyone can clearly see it. Often is the case that the audience sees it and it's plain that everyone in the film should be seeing it, but for some reason unknown to the audience, they're oblivious. With Notorious you can clearly see via facial expressions, inquiring dialogue, and mannerisms that anyone close enough sees there's something more going on than simply a professional relationship. Not one of my favorite Hitchcock films (I've never been much for a simple romance film) lovers of the genre should find plenty of enjoyment of this happy ending tale.</fullReview>

At times, a bit slow, but Hitchcock really knew what he was doing and was a master of his craft.</fullReview>

An example is his ending that leaves you guessing.</fullReview>

		<username>Scott Amundsen</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Ingrid Bergman's Greatest Performance...But Where Was The Academy?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The 1946 Oscars finally recognized the talents of Olivia de Havilland, who had been laboring in Hollywood for more than a decade, watching her less-talented younger sister Joan Fontaine get better parts than she was getting, and winning the Oscar first in the process, while de Havilland (with the exception of GONE WITH THE WIND) had to fight against her radiant beauty as Warner Bros kept using her as arm candy for Errol Flynn.The film she won for was TO EACH HIS OWN, a rather moldy soap opera about a woman who has a child out of wedlock and then spends the rest of the film trying not to tell the young man she is his mother.It's a swell performance, but the movie is not worthy of her; the story was dated even when it was made, and today it is hopelessly antiquated and de Havilland is too good for the movie and upsets its dramatic balance as a result. She would win a well-deserved Oscar three years later for THE HEIRESS, and made a number of other films in this period that would have been more worthy, like THE DARK MIRROR and THE SNAKE PIT.All this digression is my way of leading up to presenting my own choice for Best Actress of 1946: the incomparable Ingrid Bergman in her greatest performance with the possible exception of AUTUMN SONATA, in Alfred Hitchcock's classic espionage tale NOTORIOUS.Bergman's image in Hollywood up to this point was squeaky clean: she played either spotless heroines (as in CASABLANCA) or innocent victims (as in GASLIGHT, which won her the Oscar that should have gone to Barbara Stanwyck for DOUBLE INDEMNITY). NOTORIOUS must have given her fans a bit of a shock: she plays Alicia Huberman, the daughter of a convicted German spy who has committed suicide in prison. Alicia is no traitor; she loves America and harbors nothing but hatred for her father. She's no angel, either: she likes to party and she drinks too much (and it is made fairly clear that she does more than that).One day after a drunken party, she awakens to find that one of her guests has not left: a man named Devlin (Cary Grant), who crashed the party and managed to prevent her getting arrested when she insisted on going for a drive three sheets to the wind and a cop stopped them.Alicia realizes this is no ordinary guy. And she is immediately suspicious of him, with good reason: he is sizing her up to take on the job of going undercover and involving herself with one of her father's compatriots (Claude Rains), an assignment that ends with her actually marrying the man. In the interim she has fallen in love with Devlin, which places the character in the (for its time) shocking position of being driven by the man she loves into another man's bed.This is one of Hitchcock's most sophisticated films. Its frank sexuality is decades ahead of its time, and Ingrid Bergman, who was already well-known as one of Hollywood's great beauties, is quite frankly sexy in this film, one of the few times she was allowed to be so overtly carnal.The film has the usual extraordinary twists and turns Hitch was so fond of, and its greatest irony is that the "hero" (Grant), despite being in love with her, is a real bastard to her for most of the film, while the Nazi criminal she eventually marries not only loves her, his love blinds him to what she is up to.NOTORIOUS has long been a favorite of mine. Ingrid Bergman was a magnificent actress who never really gave a bad performance, but Alicia Huberman was one of her greatest roles and I shall never understand why the Academy did not even see fit to give her a nomination.I should add, in closing, that Bergman is aided and abetted here by one of the best casts Hitch ever assembled. Cary Grant delivers the goods here in a fully fleshed-out performance; his love for Alicia makes him bitter when she goes through with the marriage to Sebastian; the sexual tension between them is incredible.Claude Rains as Alex Sebastian is perfect: probably no other actor in Hollywood could have played a Nazi in 1946 and actually make him sympathetic. And Leopoldine Konstantin as Alex's mother is not only right up there with Angela Lansbury's chilling Mrs Iselin in THE MANCHURIAN CANDIDATE for sheer evil, but the role was offered first to Ethel Barrymore, who declined it, making her possibly the most fortuitous "second choice" since Judy Garland won the role of Dorothy because Zanuck refused to loan out Shirley Temple to MGM.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Bergman as the romantic spy - a classic Hitchcock thriller</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Notorious is a 1946 espionage film directed by Alfred Hitchcock, and stars Cary Grant, Ingrid Bergman, and Claude Rains. The story takes place just after the end of WWII. While the Allies have one the war, there is still concern of Nazi influence. The film opens with Alicia Huberman (Bergman) watching her father be convicted of treason in a federal courtroom; he does not atone for his actions and dies shortly thereafter. Devlin (Grant) is a CIA agent that enlists Alicia to spy for the US government on her father's friends. At first she is reluctant but then agrees. Alicia befriends her father's closest friend, Alexander Sebastian (Rains) and begins a romantic relationship with him. Now Devlin must manage Alicia in a much more complex situation, while trying to restrain his own passionate interests with her.Hitchcock is a master at building suspense and intrigue in films. Notorious provides an excellent example of how Hitchcock can take a mundane event ? the depletion of wine bottles at a party ? and turn it into a suspenseful event for the movie audience. The scene is the large dinner party; Alicia is trying to sneak Devlin into Alexander's wine cellar where Devlin hopes to find clues on Nazi activities. Hitchcock constantly focuses on the open bar where wine is being served, and one observes the stock supply of wine dwindling. Will Devlin be able to inspect the wine cellar before Alexander needs to obtain more wine? Notorious has a run time of 101 minutes, and provides a compelling story. The characters of Alicia and Alexander are well developed, but this reviewer felt that additional time was needed to develop Grant's character.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Unequaled Hitchcock Espionage Thriller Classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Notorious is a thriller film directed and produced by Alfred Hitchcock that features Cary Grant, Ingrid Bergman and Claude Rains as three people whose lives become intimately entangled during an espionage operation.It is about an American daughter of a convicted Nazi spy that is recruited by a government agent to infiltrate a group of Germans living in Brazil after the war.Alicia Huberman, the American daughter of a convicted Nazi spy, is recruited by government agent T. R. Devlin to infiltrate an organization of Nazis who have relocated to Brazil after World War II Devlin is ordered to enlist the libidinous Alicia's aid in trapping Alexander Sebastian, the head of a Brazilian neo-Nazi group. Openly contemptuous of Alicia despite her loyalty to the American cause, Devlin calmly instructs her to woo and wed Sebastian, so that that good guys will have an "inside woman" to monitor the Nazi chieftain's activities. It is only after Alicia and Sebastian are married that Devlin admits to himself that he's fallen in love with her. The "MacGuffin" in this case is a cache of uranium ore, hidden somewhere on Sebastian's estate. Upon discovering that his wife is a spy, Sebastian balks at eliminating her until ordered to do so by his virago of a mother. Tension mounts to a fever pitch as Devlin, a day late and several dollars short, strives to rescue Alicia from Sebastian's homicidal designs. Of the several standout sequences, the film's highlight is an extended love scene between Alicia and Devlin, which manages to ignite the screen while still remaining scrupulously within the edicts of the Production Code. In later years, Hitchcock never tired of relating the story of how he and screenwriter Ben Hecht fell under the scrutiny of the FBI after electing to use uranium as a plot device which was futuristic as this was made before the bombing of Hiroshima and Nagasaki.Hitchcock's Notorious is the most elegant expression of the master's visual style, just as Vertigo is the fullest expression of his obsessions.Also,it is characterized by a richly dense meditation on romantic love, featuring exquisite performances by Ingrid Bergman and Cary Grant.Added to that,it is a nearly flawless espionage thriller that has been mimicked many times since, but never equaled. This makes this a bona-fide classic worthy of a watching at present.</fullReview>

		<username>ruiresende84 (ruiresende84@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>pre-rope</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hitchcock is one of the most important directors ever, someone who changed film rules, film codes, who introduced a huge number of new terms to film grammar.He mastered and built new things around cinematic uses for camera movement, framing, and he bent narrative in marvelous brilliant ways. His top achievements can and must be studied by us today, they were the milestone for much of what followed, and in narrative terms, some things that he has done are still unsurpassed.But, even if today, i look at really old Hitchcock films and detect in them bits and pieces of what his intuition might be getting at, I really believe that the 50's were the the decade when he developed all the things for which i love him today, and believe him to be one of the masters. With Rope comes the first time in which he really builds something totally new, in that case bending camera movement, creating a cinematic eye, bright and new. Dial M... Rear Window, Vertigo, even Psycho. All those are works which you have to see.But before Rope, what we have are hints. In this film, there are a few bits of framing and camera movement which are really cleverly and conceived. The coffee cup framed while a dialogue is going on. The crane shot that begins opened to the house lobby space, and closes on Bergman's hand, holding the key. Those are really nice, and do something very hitchcockian: a scene where apparently nothing relevant happens (a trifle dialog, the simple arrival of guests), but through the camera movement and framing, a new meaning is given to a detail of the scene. Purely visual, few people worked as a purely visual mind as well as H.. But the grand picture, here and in nearly every film before Rope, is just not that great. As noir, this fails, because the world of this film is explained all the way, it's a simple spy story, which we follow based on the tension of the "is she gonna get caught?" scenes. Noir would require a bizarre unexplained world, something about us not knowing what's happening. This is a "mcguffin" type of construction, that stuff about the wine bottles, which are only good to make us want to follow. And Hitchcock always mastered that device, but his best results come when he uses that distraction to deliver us an incredible visual presentation for it. Not here.We do have Grant and Bergman, a hot couple back than. They do deliver their performance well enough. They do exhale some cinematic magic. And Ingrid was a real woman, and a real actress. But this film is mostly based upon style. And style, we know, fades in no time. So this film gives us not much today, because the master hadn't yet reached the perfection of his later cinematic manipulations of our visual minds. A few times before Rope he was close to achieve that. But here, he's just trying out a few solutions, but this film is a pure exercise in style, a style that is no longer the one we look for today.But Ingrid Bergman was some kind of a woman.My opinion: 2/5http://www.7eyes.wordpress.com</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A superb text book film, but with very little to teach about life and love.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Notorious" is a textbook film for using dramatic irony to structure a story. Unfortunately, like the rest of Hitchcock's films, it left me emotionally cold. The film has a great deal to teach about screen-writing and film making, but very little to teach about life and love.It's a poor love story, and a poor spy story - the genre beats for these types of story are mostly missing from the film. It's the dramatic irony that makes it fascinating - different characters having different levels of knowledge at different times. All the main turning points revolve around a character having a "realisation" and catching up with what the audience already knows. Each moment of realisation creates a wonderful sense of peril and intrigue that are sustained right through the next sequence of scenes until the next realisation.Ingrid Bergman is astonishing to watch - her face tells us every thought she has. The film is slanted so that it's her story, rather than Cary Grant's.Is it a good film? No. But its a decidedly wonderful film to watch for writers, film makers, and Ingrid Bergman fans.</fullReview>

		<date>2009-08-09</date>
		<username>theowinthrop</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>What might be in a champagne bottle</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It is unique in the Hitchcock pantheon, for something he rarely did - he made his "MacGuffin" mean something for a change.Made just as World War II ended, NOTORIOUS (like Orson Welles' THE STRANGER) picked up on the theme of what were yesterday's bogeymen (the Nazis) up to after they suffered the 1945 military defeat. There was a general suspicion that certain Latin American states (read Argentina, Paraguay) were havens for war criminals fleeing justice in Europe. This was true (we now know that Eichmann and Dr. Mengele were among those criminals who hid there for decades). But the issue was were they simply nursing their wounds or planning more evil actions in the future.The general feeling was that Nazis who fled remained Nazis, and would be preparing for a future come-back. This actually never happened (neither the Peron Government in Argentina nor the Stroessner Regime in Paraguay felt inclined to do more than protect these people on their soil. Argentina had potential as a major state, but it was still in a state of development. Paraguay was among the poorest countries in the world. But people were aware that there were vast untapped natural resources throughout South America, and large German communities in most of the countries. It was easy to jump to the conclusion that the fleeing Nazis would be trying to develop (in "secret") all kinds of weapons.From what he later said, Hitchcock had picked up on the subject of uranium in his reading. He always claimed that he had little real knowledge of it's use in atomic weapons. One finds that a little hard to totally believe, but in this one film he did use a sensible "MacGuffin" (his term for a secret that spins the plot of his stories along, which he thought nobody would really be interested in). He would have members of "Farben" (obviously the I.G.Farben company from Germany) in Brazil working on getting bottles full of uranium out of the mines of that country.The film itself is remarkable for the love triangle of Cary Grant, Ingrid Bergman, and Claude Rains. Grant is a Federal agent (O.S.S.?) who recruits Bergman for the government so she can distant herself from her father, an unrepentant Nazi agent convicted of treason. Grant realizes that Bergman is trying to clean her image up from being a party-loving slut, and the two do gradually fall in love. But the nature of her mission (to romance Rains, who formerly dated her) strains the relationship between them: she thinks he could have objected to his bosses (Louis Calhern and Moroni Olsen) choosing her for the job, and he thinks she could have turned it down flat if she really had changed. Rains problems are that he does love Bergman (who he does not suspect of double dealing with him), but is under the thumb of his mother (Madame Konstantyne) who has always disliked her "rivals" for her sons affections, but is particularly suspicious of Bergman turning up again.Hitchcock got some excellent film moments here, the two best being the "longest" kissing section of any film of the 1940s, and the suspense building section at a party in Rains' home, when Bergman and Grant are examining the wine cellar, while Rains' butler is keeping an eye on the declining number of champagne bottles in his ice bucket. The conclusion, when the principals are leaving the mansion for a car, and Rains' associates are watching is also a great conclusion, with it's ominous overtones as it ends.I will add one final point...as mentioned earlier the business about the champagne bottles and the uranium is a running theme in the film, but oddly enough Hitchcock did use champagne in another sequence - when Grant is going to have a cooked chicken dinner with Bergman, he buys a bottle of champagne. But in the moment of shock when he learns what her assignment will be, he leaves the bottle behind (absent minded), and subsequently recalls it at the end of his mutual recrimination scene with Bergman, when they find that the chicken dinner is cold. Then Grant discovers he does not know where the champagne bottle is. It is like Hitchcock had champagne bottles on his mind throughout this film.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Vovion</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>A well acted, intelligent film hindered by a disappointing end.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Notorious is one of Hitchcock's most greatly revered films. In knowing this I tried to stay away from reviews and comments before launching into it, in order to try to keep my first viewing pure from outside influence. The plot is set up effectively in the first half an hour, presenting our two heroes Devlin (Grant) and Alicia (Bergman) as people who while come from very different backgrounds but share an impenetrable bond from when they first meet till the end of the film. This relationship these two characters share is the finest thing about 'Notorious', the chemistry between Grant and Bergman and the story of unrequited love they tell through it is a mastery of subtlety and gesture, particularly towards the end of the film. When without Grant, Bergman also shines playing Alicia, a woman who is thrust into a situation where she has to covertly investigate Alexander Sebastian; an accused traitor who the government believe Alicia can gain access to. While this in itself creates a successful thrilling plot device it also works very well as a comment of dehumanisation. To those in authority she is nothing more than tool to gain information and they urge her on to constantly put herself in dangerous situations to get the much needed evidence. When they finally have what they need, she is essentially discarded from their concern, even when she goes missing for an extended period of time.The direction from Hitchcock is true to his form, well paced, controlled and when necessary intense. The primary flaw with the film I would suggest is the ending. One could argue it was leaved in such an open manner in order for the audience to fill in what happens next, but because the final ten minutes are so romantic, intense and suspenseful it feels more anti climactic. Sometimes using this technique for the final of a film works well, but if feels like the story and characters need more explanation sometimes it can do substantial damage to a picture and I believe this is the case with Notorious. It felt like the film was just getting to the core of the relationship between Grant and Bergman and also the intricate relationship between Sebastian and his partners. But as both are becoming more visible and interesting, they are abruptly severed, leaving the audience not necessarily seeking resolution but further development.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Notorious</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I think this film has just etched out a few of Hitchcock's other films of my all time Hitchcock films. ( 39 Steps still being #1) It is a film that honestly never lets up one bit and it contains one fantastic performance after another. The film's two stars: Cary Grant and Ingrid Bergman also share a chemistry together that is just a must see for any actor, or movie fan. The basic plot of the film finds Alicia Huberman (Ingrid Bergman) going undercover to infiltrate a group of Nazi's who have relocated to Brazil after World War II. Cary Grant plays US Government Agent Devlin, Alicia's handler, who is ordered to convince her to marry Sebastian (Claude Rains), one of her convicted Nazi father's friends and one of her former lovers, to find out what he's plotting. But just how far will Alicia go in this assignment, and how far will Devlin allow her to go? This is what is at the heart of Alfred Hitchcock's Notorious. Tough yet very vulnerable Cary Grant has never been better. As Devlin, he hides true feelings for Alicia the entire film. This is the theme of love vs. duty done at it's best: as Devlin is torn between his true feelings for Alicia and his perceived duty. The entire film, he can't reconcile his love for Alicia with his own fear of losing her or with his own self-loathing. I think one of the greatest scenes of Grant's acting career is in a scene-in which his CIA superior disparages Alicia for her apparent lack of "character" she has shown by following the same very own orders Devlin and his superiors have given her. Lashing out Devlin simply says: "She may be risking her life, but when it comes to being a *lady,* she doesn't hold a candle to your wife, sir, sitting in Washington playing bridge with three other ladies of great honor and virtue." Also, how in the hell did Ingrid Bergman manage to be skipped out of the Oscar's for this movie? She wasn't even nominated! What? The range of her performance throughout the entire film is astonishing... vulnerable, strong, and simply gorgeous Bergman is simply brilliant. Not to mention that pout she does...... There are so many moments I could list as my favorite. But, I wouldn't even know where to start. So, I'll just mention some of the scenes that just knocked me silly. The first one, is the scene where Sebastian realizes that something is up and he goes to his mother (Leopoldine Konstantin) confessing that " I'm married to an American Agent." Konstantin's acting in this scene is so chilling, jeez. She basically says nothing except for a smile of grim satisfaction (she was right about that rival for her son's affections after all) grabs a cigarette and lights it up. The second is the scene where Alicia realizes she has just been poisoned and Hitchcock just centers on a huge close up of Sebastian and his mother. Brilliant. "Darling. What is it?" And lastly, is probably the scene of the film where Devlin carries a very poisoned Alicia out of the mansion in full view of the conspirators, leaving the hapless Sebastian to the non-existent mercy of his "friends". "Alex, will you come in, please? I wish to talk to you." "If you only once had said that you loved me. Oh, Dev." "Listen. You chalked up another boyfriend. That's all. No harm done." "I hate you." "There's no occasion to. You're doing good work. Number 10's out in front. It looks as if Sebastian knows how to pick 'em." "Is that all you have to say to me?" "Dry your eyes baby. It's out of character. Keep on your toes. There's a tough job around. Snap out of it. Here comes dreamboat." "I'm afraid I-I can't make it because they gave me pills to sleep." "Keep awake. Keep talking..." "They didn't want the others to know about me." "Keep talking. Come on, what happened? What happened?" "Alex found out." "And the others haven't?" "They'd kill him if they knew. They killed Emil." "Are you in pain?" "I don't know - the pills...Say it again, it keeps me awake." "I love you. Stand up. Stand up. Come on, wake up. Talk!" "Don't ever leave me." "You'll never get rid of me again." "I never tried to." "No room Sebastian." "But you must take me, they're watching me." "That's your headache." 		<shortDescription>I wish to talk to you!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There was something very minimalistic about this film as it started out.But as the story progressed, especially in the 1 hour mark, Hitchcock performs his usual sleight of hand as a filmmaker. From the best stylized POV shots, to the foreground shot of the evil coffee cup that you see in many film-making textbooks today.But all throughout the film is a study of subjectivity vs. objectivity in storytelling, most of the time we are privvy to what goes on in our hero, and especially our anti-heroine Bergman's mind, but then that privy-ness of information is then passed on to our antagonists - yet audiences still in the know. And that is where the suspense comes from.Grant is as suave as always. This is definitely better than To Catch a Thief, but I am having trouble deciding whether I should rank it above North By Northwest (which had better storytelling, imho).NOTORIOUS is all about subtletly, in suspense, in exposition, despite the presence of Hitchcock's cinematic wizardry in it. The ending has one of the most quiet yet intense scenes where our 2 main characters come to terms and realizations. And despite the darker tone this one has over "Northwest" or even "Rear Window", I'm glad it doesn't end morbidly like Psycho or Vertigo. Spoilers for those films there.NOTORIOUS is 5/5 in my book.</fullReview>

		<username>andrewsarchus</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A wonderfully structured film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Notorious is definitely one of Hitchcock's best films. The topicality of the atom bomb set against the eternal theme of love and redemption make the film timeless. I was particularly struck by the symmetry of the first and last scenes. The movie begins and ends behind the door of courtroom in the opening shot and what has become a courtroom for Sebastian in the final shot. Similarly the recruitment by Devlin of the hung-over Alicia at the beginning is mirrored by his rescue of the poisoned Alicia at the end - similar POV low angle shots and lighting. In between these bookends there is all the great stuff written about in these comments- the extended, claustrophobic kisses, the I-hurt-you/you-hurt-me relationship of Devlin and Alicia, the extraordinary acting from all, and possibly the best line in cinema from Mrs Sebastian - "We are protected by the enormity of your stupidity. For a time." One other interesting note- Selznick sold the contract of Hitchcock to RKO in order to finance his out of control production of the rather horrible Duel in the Sun, his paean to his then new flame Jennifer Jones. RKO gave Hitchcock much more freedom than he ever had with Selznick as a producer and he came though with a masterpiece. After Notorious he completed his contract for Selznick (who had brought him over from England) with the very mediocre The Paradine Case. All this was covered in a wonderful PBS American Masters episode on the two men. Free of Selznick, he then went on to direct a series of popular films in the 50's and 60's that I think are unequaled by any director in their quality and impact on the culture.</fullReview>

		<date>2006-05-03</date>
		<username>robb_772</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Hitchcock's greatest film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The definitive film of suspense legend Alfred Hitchcock, NOTORIOUS is one of the purest exercises of grueling suspense that has ever been captured on celluloid. Few films can successfully mesh political intrigue with gloomy romance and top it off with a slowly-building murder plot, but Hitchcock effortlessly pulls off the near impossible by managing to weave all of the various plot threads into one haunting tragedy. This is a truly character driven film - although the containing numerous examples of the masterful set pieces that Hitchcock is known for, the technique never overshadows the film's characters, who emerge as living human beings rather than shallow plot contrivances. Arguably, viewers make a stronger emotional connection to NOTORIOUS' characters than that of any other Hitchcock film.Having already been Hollywood's most successful male star for more than a decade at the time, Cary Grant took a considerable gamble when he accepted the role NOTORIOUS' anti-hero, T. R. Devlin. Grant had flirted with playing potentially unsympathetic characters before in Howard Hawks' HIS GIRL Friday (1940) and Hitchcock's SUSPICION (1941), but never before had the charismatic actor been so willing to risk sex symbol image on such an embittered characterization. For perhaps the only time in his lengthy career, Cary Grant truly plays a vile misogynist - and the actor has seldom been more brilliant. Mr. Grant has long been my very favorite motion picture actor, so it is with considerable praise that I proclaim that NOTORIOUS is the best showcase for his monumental talent.No stranger to controversy herself, Ingrid Bergman is every bit Grant's match in a conflicted portrayal of a woman who is perpetually misused by her father, her lover, and even her own government. Bergman's Alicia is arguably Hitchcock's most tragic heroine, and the complex character is perfectly suited to Bergman's incomparable strengths as an actress. A faultless blend of strength with vulnerability and love with hatred, Bergman is one of the only thespians (living or dead) who could pull off such a difficult balancing act with falling into caricature. In addition to the mesmerizing leads, the film is further enhanced by a flawless supporting cast, particularly the gravely-voiced Claude Rains in a chilling-yet-sympathetic performance as a Nazi spy, and a bravura turn by Leopoldine Konstantin as the silver screen's most sinister mother.The film has several of the elaborate set-pieces that Hitchcock built his reputation on, and they all rank among the director's best. Alicia and Devlin's discovery of mineral deposits in Sebastian's wine cellar, Sebastian noticing the re-appearance of a particular key on his key ring, and Alicia's horrifying realization of exactly what is occurring to her are all classic moments in Hitchcock's massive cannon of films. Even more impressively, so much of the action is carried out in complete silence without relying on the benefit of dialogue to explain the on screen occurrences. Few directors posses the necessary skill that Hitchcock displays in creating sequences like these, and they all come together perfectly in NOTORIOUS - which is the director's greatest achievement.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>desh79</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Or: How Hitchcock turned an average script into a good film (again)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I think this film underlines precisely why Hitchcock is, justifiably, regarded as one of the all-time greatest directors. With Notorious he basically took a fairly average script and turned what could have been a lacklustre film noir into an involving thriller, full of Hitchcock's trademark apprehension and suspense. All the Hitchcockian devices are there - the MacGuffin, those eerie pans and focuses on suspect devices (though, curiously, the heroine is not a blonde for a change but Hitchcock's one-time object of desire, the one and only Ingrid Bergman).Say what you like about Hitchcock, he understood, possibly better than many of his contemporaries, that the script itself is merely the skeleton of a film. Neglect the rest, ie. camera, sound, editing, casting, etc, and you still end up with only less than half a body of work. The story itself is nothing special; it's the techniques Hitchcock used to tell the story which matter and which draw you into the narrative. While the set admittedly does look somewhat outdated (Grant and Bergman obviously never went anywhere near Rio De Janeiro, instead being cast in front of a screen), you can't fault Hitchcock or anyone else directly involved in the making of this film for constraints put upon them by film-making procedures of the day. In other words, blame the studio system, not the cast or the director.Notorious, alongside Rebecca, was the movie which firmly established Hitchcock in the Hollywood elite and gave him the platform on which he could create his more experimental works, starting with Rope and moving on to Rear Window and Vertigo, and in a way it can be seen as part of the bridge between Hitchcock's noir-ish work of the 1940s and what was, to all intents and purposes, his avantgarde period of the 1950s. In other words, it's a benchmark film.Recommended to fans of the director (though I'll suspect most of them will have seen Notorious already) and 1940s film noir in general.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>silverscreen888</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A Brilliant Plot line; A Fine Production; Bergman is Wonderful Here</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a film with a very absorbing plot line, Ingrid Bergman's interesting character brought to life by her luminous performance, and solid but unspectacular technical achievements. The wonder of it, for me, is that it is a film as watchable as it is. Alfred Hitchcock did not invent but did advance the non-crime noir film--the film about a central character whose ethical purpose places him beyond the help of those authorities and agencies whose defined job it is to help a citizen like him. The character played by Bergman, Miss Huberman, is recruited by Cary Grant's agency in the film to do a job for them, to erase her father's guilt in espionage matters. When against his will agent Devlin, played by Grant, finds himself forgiving her past , caring about her and falling in love with her, the already-complex task she has been assigned, marrying the bad guy played by Claude Rains and finding out how he has been committing his crimes--and getting out alive--becomes all the harder for both of them. In the cast besides Grant who does better than one might expect and Miss Bergman are Rains, Leopoldine Konstantine as his abominable mother, solid Moroni Olsen, Louis Calhern, evil Reinhold Schunzel, Fay Baker and many others in smaller parts. Cinematography is by Ted Tetzlaff, a noted director himself, music by prolific Roy Webb. The sets by Claude E. Carpenter and Darrell Silvera and Edith Head's wardrobe for Bergmann are outstanding. But the film does not have the spaciousness nor the flow of Hitchcock's best. What is does have is a fine, taut and well-crafted script by Ben Hecht, Clifford Odetts and director Hitchcock, that makes all the difference. The pace is that of a drama, but the action is that of a clever mystery story, featuring gradual revelations, tense scenes between a doubting Devlin and a bitter Huberman and a bang-up climax and ending satisfying to all. What keeps it from greatness is the idea that Grant won't love Bergman and nearly gets them both killed because of that failing. But this is a B/W gem, even better than its reputation, a film worthy of many screenings, in my judgment.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Anyone wondering why Alfred Hitchcock got his reputation as "the master of suspense" has only to view NOTORIOUS to see how he directs two of the biggest box-office stars of the 1940s in his suspenseful spy classic.Basically,it's the story of twisted love with Bergman marrying Nazi (Claude Rains) in order to infiltrate herself into his plans against the U.S. while falling in love with the American agent (Grant) who gave her the mission. His extended kissing scene with Bergman that begins on an outdoor terrace and ends by a wall telephone is typical Hitchcock romanticism.As usual, it is Claude Rains who turns up the menace at an appropriate moment. His sudden discovery that Bergman's marriage to him is a sham is conveyed in a chilling close-up that reveals the crafty plan he has in mind for her demise.If you're a fan of Hitchcock, Grant or Bergman, you can't afford to miss NOTORIOUS. An added plus is Roy Webb's effective background score, lushly romantic while setting the tone for suspense.</fullReview>

Grant and Bergman are magic and I especially love her as the drunk partygiver/driver out to have a picnic. Rains brings 3 dimensions to his character (at least!). Interesting parallels between many simultaneous levels of interpretation: i.e. appearance, reality, and what one believes of oneself. This is my personal favorite Hitchcock movie; I'm not really a big Hitchcock fan, because he tends to be too removed from the emotions involved and too in love with the images he projects. 		<date>2002-08-12</date>
		<username>gaityr</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Engaging, Suspenseful, Brilliant!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Films just don't come like this one anymore. First of all, NOTORIOUS is directed by Alfred Hitchcock--which is always a good reason to see a film, and often one's only reason to pick it up in the first place. NOTORIOUS is a stunning work of art: no one made black and white work for the film and the actors as much as Hitchcock did; Ingrid Bergman has never looked lovelier, nor Cary Grant handsomer. Some of the shots, such as Alicia (Bergman) looking up at Devlin (Grant) from the bed, are so simple and yet wonderfully effective. The way Hitchcock's camera closes in tightly on the faces of Alicia and Devlin during passionate scenes adds a new level of intimacy to their relationship that one can't get even with the best actors in the business (and that's what Bergman and Grant were). In other words, his direction--the placement of an object, the reaction or long shot of another character--isn't of the 'hey, take a look at me' variety. It's unobstrusive and subtle, and yet one can't help noticing it while watching the film. Second of all, the film stars Ingrid Bergman and Cary Grant, Bergman in surely what is one of her finest roles (and I'm saying this with NOTORIOUS being the first film of hers I've seen to date). The love story between Alicia and Devlin happens so quickly that one really couldn't believe it if Bergman didn't play a girl in love so very convincingly. The look on her face each time Devlin refuses to speak up for her in her presence (he has no problem doing so when she's not around!) is positively heartbreaking. And since it is also difficult to believe that anyone could fail to fall in love with Ingrid Bergman, who surely must have one of the most luminous and photographable faces in the history of film, Cary Grant doesn't have all that much work to do with his slightly mysterious character of an intelligence agent named Devlin (whose first name we and Alicia never learn). His role is decidely a supporting one for Bergman to really show her stuff, and like the gentleman he is, he plays the role he's given and doesn't try to steal her scenes. That said, he really is just perfect in the role, and every time Dev pretends that Alicia's dates and marriage with Alex (Claude Rains) don't bother him, it's easy to see his heart break just a little more beneath the cool undisturbed surface. Bergman and Grant together are, of course, electric, from their first kiss to their passionate scene in the Sebastians' bedroom. ("Tell me again. It keeps me awake.") Watching the actors acting in this film is almost a good enough reason to watch this film again... almost only because the whole film itself provides the best reason one could have.Finally, NOTORIOUS doesn't have a shoddy script--to the contrary, it's full of great lines and dialogue, even though the plot itself has now become quite typical of spy-thrillers. (Girl becomes intelligence agent, falls in love with her contact etc. etc.) It still feels fresh with this film though, and one still cares deeply about what happens to the characters. The dialogue is quietly witty, peppered with little things that make the viewer smile but don't detract from the atmosphere simultaneously being built up by the film. In fact, one never knows what kind of people Devlin and Alicia really are; what information you have you pick up from conversations between the characters, so in the end all you really know is that Devlin and Alicia are in love, and that's all that matters. Do yourself a favour and watch NOTORIOUS, preferably knowing as little about it as possible (I've tried to keep my review more or less spoiler-free). It won't take you long to fall in love with it--it really is quite a film for so little story.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Romance 10, thrills 7</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have to disagree with the previous reviewer that this is an underrated Hitchcock film. In fact, it regularly pops up on "best" lists. If anything, I would say it is somewhat overrated. It is certainly the talkiest of Hitch's great films. A perfunctory "nest of spies" story takes a back seat to the strange love triangle of Cary Grant, Ingrid Berman, and Claude Rains. This latter element is brilliantly played by the three stars and features typical Hitchcock irony: the man she loves (Grant) doesn't trust Bergman, while the man who loves her (Rains) trusts her too much. The film also includes two classic sequences, both involving the key to wine cellar, and one of Hitch's memorable supporting characters, Rains's mother. It also includes one of the screen's greatest kisses, by Grant and Bergman. As a love story, it gets a 10; as a thriller, a 7.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>For me, it's a flawless film--camera-work, story, acting, and editing are superb</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Notorious (1946)Such a tumultuous, ever-changing plot, filled with real romance and political intrigue, filmed with visual panache, and acted to perfection. What is there to not like here? It strikes me as a perfect movie, really. Okay, yes, I don't like one line the Brazilian police official offers a meeting. And Cary Grant can't, as usual, kiss like her really means it. But this is nitpicking a really full blooded, interesting, beautiful movie.Alfred Hitchcock was always a little proud that he foresaw the importance of uranium as he was filming the movie, and along with "Detour" it is among the first movies in an atomic age to confront the problem. Ingrid Bergman acts her heart out here, and is more than a match for both Cary Grant and Claude Rains. This might be when Hitchcock said to her, "Ingrid, it's just a movie." Which says a lot about both of them--Hitchcock, wanting to remind everyone that movies are an entertainment, a commercial product, and Bergman wanting to show that movies were an outlet for the most intense kind of acting. By the way, the writing is by the incomparable Ben Hecht.The film was shot in the U.S., as usual, with lots of back projected scenes of Rio to make it seem like it was Brazil, where the story was set. Most of this is fairly convincing, but in usual Hitchcock fashion, I don't think he fully cared, as long as you got the idea. (This is a little like the backdrops on a stage set, where you know fully well it's all a sham, but you go along with it because that's how theater works.) The famous kiss between Grant and Bergman outside the hallway by the wine cellar got around the Hays Code rules about passionate kisses by having them stop and start kissing over and over. This makes it even sexier, really, even though they never quite actually kiss--even Bogart and Bergman have better kisses in "Casablanca."</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>"You're sore because you've fallen for a little drunk you tamed in Miami and you don't like it. It makes you sick all over, doesn't it? This is going to be one of my gushing reviews, because I absolutely loved Notorious. If you'd rather not sit and read through a few paragraphs of nothing but praise, you can stop now.&nbsp;I thought I had seen the best of what Hitchcock had to offer. I had already watched most of his most popular movies, and the last few lesser known films of his that I had watched were good, but not great. I expected Notorious to be of similar quality. I was completely wrong. By the end of the movie, it had jumped right up to third on my list, behind only Rear Window and Psycho. It's just that good.Notorious is&nbsp;essentially a spy-thriller with an excellent, mature love story weaved throughout. I won't reveal many details, to preserve the story, but it's one of the very best of its kind. A wanton party girl (and daughter of a recently uncovered Nazi traitor) is pressed into service for the U.S. and used to spy on a sinister but nebulous Nazi plot in Brazil. That may sound like familiar territory for the genre, but most of the films that came later and copied it don't manage to be nearly as fresh and well-made as this movie from 1946.Two more things absolutely have to be mentioned, and I'll be done: Ingrid Bergman's performance and the ending. This was my introduction to Ingrid, and holy $@*!, this lady could act! I finally understand her inevitably high position in any credible list of the best actresses in the history of Hollywood. The majority of the acting burden rests on her shoulders, and she pulls it off effortlessly. Her character is completely different in the beginning, middle, and end of the movie, and she's consistently excellent throughout. I'll be seeing more of her work, that's for sure.As for the finale, it's one of the most tense, suspenseful, menacing and masterful that I've ever seen - and there's nary a gunshot or an explosion to be had. There's not even much of a confrontation. If you want to know why Aldred Hitchcock is considered to be one of the greatest directors ever, watch that sequence.If you love spy movies, you must watch Notorious. If you love classic movies, you must watch Notorious. If you want to love classic movies, you must watch Notorious. Basically, you must watch Notorious. Watch it.</fullReview>

		<date>2009-08-09</date>
		<username>seymourblack-1</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The Cruel Costs Of Love &amp; Duty</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In "Notorious", a troubled atmosphere grows as the audience's expectations are repeatedly defied. What seems to begin as an espionage thriller rapidly develops into an intense and twisted love story. An elegant visual style is used to portray acts of deception, betrayal, vicious plotting and attempted murder. An apparently honourable and patriotic man conspires to deliver the woman he loves into the arms of another man and an important member of a Nazi spy ring is actually a charming, sincere and sympathetic character. Even more strangely, the daughter of a convicted Nazi spy, who has a reputation for promiscuity, partying and drinking exploits, agrees to take part in a U.S. Government assignment which she knows will place her in mortal danger. The unsettling impact of all this is also complemented visually by the use of camera angles which seem to disrupt any natural symmetry and some unorthodox shots such as those which focus on the back of the main protagonists' heads.The conflicts encountered by characters who have to choose between patriotic duty and love, the inherent lack of trust that exists between people who are actually in love with each other and the complicated motivations involved, all serve to create a palpable sense of tension which is further heightened by the desperately dangerous and life threatening positions they frequently find themselves in.After the father of Alicia Huberman (Ingrid Bergman) is convicted of treason, she hosts a party where she meets T.R. Devlin (Cary Grant). She soon realises that this is no chance meeting and Devlin, who is a U.S. Intelligence Agent, offers her a job which involves infiltrating a group of Nazis in Brazil to gather information about their activities. She agrees to go to Rio with Devlin and during the days immediately after they arrive there, the couple enjoy each other's company and gradually fall in love. Things change however, after Devlin is called to a meeting with his superior officer who explains that the proposed assignment would require Alicia to seduce Alexander Sebastian (Claude Rains) so that she could be well placed to discover the precise nature of the group's activities.When Devlin returns to Alicia, he adopts a much colder and more distant manner and then explains what would be required of her. She becomes uncertain and seeks his advice. He, possibly for professional or patriotic reasons, refuses to oblige and she presumes that this is a sign that he doesn't genuinely love her and so she accepts the job.Sebastian had been a friend of Alicia's father and it was well known that he'd had a crush on her. This made Alicia's task relatively easy and in a very short time, she marries Sebatian as planned and after returning from the honeymoon, quickly familiarises herself with the layout of their new home. She is able to gain access to all areas of the mansion except the wine cellar and discovers from the butler that Sebastian has the only key. After reporting back to Devlin, she agrees to throw a party, which he attends, and she takes the key from her husband's keyring and passes it to Devlin so that he can slip away and explore the contents of the cellar. This leads to the discovery of a bottle in which a mineral (later identified as uranium) is stored.Although the operation is successful, Sebastian realises almost immediately what has happened and recognises the part which Alicia has obviously played in the plot. He's unable to tell the other members of his group who are utterly ruthless, as he's certain that they would readily "eliminate" him for such a mistake, so he seeks advice from his mother. A plan is devised to poison Alicia and eventually her deteriorating health alerts Devlin to the level of danger that she's in and the course of action that he then takes, brings matters to a dramatic and powerful conclusion.A remarkable amount of drinking is featured in the story, primarily because of Alicia's predilection for consuming copious amounts of alcohol but also alka-seltzer is seen beside her as she wakes up with a hangover and she also goes on to consume numerous cups of poisoned coffee in Sebastian's mansion. In his cameo appearance Alfred Hitchcock is also seen drinking champagne.A notable feature of Hitchcock's direction is the prominence that's given to certain important props such as the coffee cups, the alka-seltzer and the key to the wine cellar. In one scene at the mansion where Alicia is holding the key in her hand ready to pass surreptitiously to Devlin, the camera descends from an extremely high starting position and in one spectacular, continuous movement zooms in to provide a very effective close-up which heightens the viewer's awareness of the level of risk that Alicia's taking.Cary Grant is typically smooth and sophisticated in his role but is also rather tense and guarded as he struggles to control the internal conflicts that are obviously plaguing his character. Ingrid Bergman is magnificent as someone who finds herself in a very unfamiliar predicament struggling with complex emotions at the same time as having to deal with the need to function in an extremely threatening environment. Claude Rains was nominated for an Academy Award for Best Supporting Actor and is excellent as a kind and vulnerable man who has to cope with a domineering mother, the betrayal of the woman he truly loves and the fear of extreme punishment for the error of judgement that he'd made.</fullReview>
	</review>
