Cary Grant and Ingrid Bergman fill the lead roles as government agent and recruited spy with their usual charm and create a shining romance. Claude Rains is skilled as usual as a Nazi leader, which he makes dangerous yet refined and even warm for the most part, creating an interesting character.This film's story is methodically paced, and overall not as engaging as other Hitchcock efforts, but for the most part is well-written. As far as a spy plot, ultimate secrets obtained and intrigue seem unimportant relative to the tension associated with infiltration, which wasn't a great detriment to the film. The tension is well-developed after the story is introduced. There are a number of excellent scenes, and in particular I liked the intriguing wine cellar sequence and the sharp ending.</fullReview>

		<username>Framescourer</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Gripping thriller thoughtfully made and acted</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There's a real sense of moral investigation in this stylish Hitchcock post-war thriller. Consciously post-war, not least because Claude Rains' former Nazi Sebastian is already a figure of dubious morality, but also as he's living in South America, to North America what Europe is to Britain, an exotic but morally confusing place.Crucially of course, the moral confusion is in and surrounds Sebastian's former wife Alicia Hubermann, played with a exotic, sexy but militant primal force by Ingrid Bergman. She drinks. Her first outfit is of European origin to affirm the symbolic allusion. She fights, in a peculiarly, almost painfully extended, sexless scene with Cary Grant, as he tries to persuade her to help him against her former husband. The sex, such as it gets represented in 50s films (i.e. by formal allusion) is arrived at after painful soul searching. And the obligatory romantic seduction that leads to it, overlooking the city, is cut as short as the earlier car struggle is long, since this time Grant wins with a conventional ease - I'm not as interested in this, says Hitchcock. Not in this movie.But many of the usual Hitchcockisms are everpresent - the cameo, the foreshot of a vital symbol (the champagne bottle), and virtuosic, almost new wave cutting to crank up the tension. The less effective moments occur when the actors have to subordinate their performances to the end idea of the Hitchcock set piece.Otherwise this is a fine matinee-style idol-as-character movie that confirmed Hitchcock's sure touch. 7/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Brillman</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Mysterious Acts Of Love By Hitchcock</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After watching Notorious by Alfred Hitchcock, many aspects of the movie caught my attention. The move started off slow building the foundation of the plot, but after Alicia and Devlin go to Rio de Janeiro, the events begin to unfold. Throughout the movie, role characterization and camera techniques were very unique and well portrayed as they work together. In the beginning of the movie, Alicia has guests over at her house showing them drinking and having a great time. She begins to talk to a man whose back is toward the audience and his face is never shown. Alicia continues to talk, but the man stays silent and unresponsive. The shot fades out and when the next scene fades in, the audience still perceives this particular man with his back towards the camera. This particular camera technique reveals underlining themes about this character. Not showing emotions and stiff behavior describes how Devlin reacts throughout the movie. After Alicia is knocked out by Devlin in the car, she waked up the next morning in a daze. When she hears a voice, Alicia looks up and we again see a man whose face is not visible. When he begins to walk towards the bed, his face becomes visible and the camera follows his body from the view of Alicia. As the camera follows Devlin, the audience realizes how Alicia's eyes stick on Devlin's face the whole time. During the party Alicia and Alex throw, the camera is used in a distinct way to show jealousy between Alex and Devlin. When Devlin arrives, the camera follows Alicia to greet Devlin. While they are talking, the shots go back and forth showing Alex's jealousy seeing out they interact. When Alicia goes back to Alex, the camera switches back and forth from Devlin to Alicia and Alex showing Devlin's jealousy as well. Finally, after Alicia realizes she has been poisoned by Alex and his mother, the camera uses their shadows to make their personas dark and wicked. Alicia begins to become dizzy and can only see the shadows outline Alex and his mother. As Alicia walks towards the door, the shadows of Alex and his mother combine showing how both personas equal one person. Overall, Notorious kept me on the edge of my seat and never let me down. The role characterization and camera techniques were connected throughout the whole movie which made this particular movie complete.</fullReview>

		<username>lucas-winter</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>One of hitchcocks greatest</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The movie screened was Notorious, by Alfred Hitchcock. The movie is unique because of its use of different camera techniques to make the viewer seem like you are really in the movie. The first example of this is at the very beginning of the movie when the camera is shown looking though a door crack as the trial is ending. Alfred Hitchcock could of chose to show the faces and the courtroom setting but didn't. The reason he did this is to make the camera more like what you would be seeing. This makes the movie believable and draws the viewer in right away. One other way in making the audience feel like they are in the movie is shot reverse shot. This is used throughout the movie but you first see it at the end of the party when the two main characters are talking. The shot reverse shot makes you feel like the main characters are addressing you. The close ups on their faces is the way you would be seeing the actor/actress if you were sitting across from them. When the two main characters are driving down the road you also see a camera shot that we have not seen so far. The camera is showing the road ahead. This is done not only to show that the lady is drunk but to give the viewer the sense that he is in the car too. Three other times when the camera is used to emulate the audience actually being in the movie is when you see through the woman's eyes when she is driving and gets hair in her eyes, when she is suffering from a hangover and her vision of the male main character rotates, and when she is drugged and her vision starts to go black before she passes out. All of these instances make you feel like you are experiencing what she is. Besides the use of camera shots to make the audience more involved with the movie. A great use of objects and camera shots were used to relay messages. The most dramatic of these is when the male main character leaves the bottle of wine behind after he gets done hearing what the assignment is for the female main character. Having the wine bottle left shows the change in the frame of mine. The male main character was filled with thoughts of the two main characters having a wonderful night together. After hearing the news every thought that he might have had is no more. Much like the wine is no longer an issue or a concern. The only thought now is them being split apart before they even come together, and the dread of what he has to tell her. One other use of this was the scene when the key reapers on the key chain. No words are said but you know what is being thought as you see the close ups of the husbands face as a close up of the key on the key ring. This close up used to build up tension is also used in the basement when the husband is looking at the bottles. A close up is used to show the importance of the shot and a sense of suspense as the camera slowly goes from one bottle to another until the date is different. Then the finding of the broken bottle and without a word the audience knows that the husband is aware his wife is a spy. The use of the close ups also is used to tell things that the actors never reveal themselves for example, it is never said the poison will be put in the coffee but you know with the first close up of the cup that is where the poison is. The use of close ups also helps to instill the false stereotype of the Germans in this movie. The Germans are depicted as cold heartless killers and this is supported by the close ups of their stone cool faces lacking any emotion. The best example of this in the movie is the end. There are quick cuts to the Nazi's faces as the two main characters are walking down the stairs. Depicting them as heartless and ruthless killers even the mother was shown to be evil. If someone had to base all of their German knowledge from this movie the person would most likely be scared to death if they had to meet one.</fullReview>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>See it on Laserdisc!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Notorious is a great movie overall, but there are editing differences between the DVD version and the laserdisc version. On the laserdisc, a film historian is narrating some scenes on the analog audio track... one that stands out is a scene in which Cary Grant's character drops a champagne/wine bottle, and the historian notes that a bottle would take less than a second to hit the floor. The laserdisc contains the bottle falling, a cut to Grant's facial expression, a cut to Ingrid Bergman's facial expression, and then a cut back to Grant's expression, then the bottle hits the floor (about 4 seconds give or take). Basically, Hitchcock extended the scene for a bit more suspense.This scene on the DVD only shows Grant's first expression and the bottle hits the floor (about the normal 1 second or so for it to fall).When it comes to Hitchcock, I'm sure he himself would have wanted the audience to see his movies exactly how he wanted them edited, and not re-edited for any reason (could you imagine re-editing of the Shower Scene in Psycho?). If the DVD is to contain different edited scenes, there should be an extra supplement stating this, not put directly into the film.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>"Notorious" is one of those movies that snares you into its web of deceit and trickery no matter how many times you've seen it. It ranks up there with "Psycho," "Rear Window" and "North by Northwest" in its ability to withstand repeated viewing.In college I took a Hitchcock Film class. Unfortunately "Notorious" wasn't on the list of films we were watching. But many of us took it upon ourselves to view and write about this beautiful film, regardless of the professor's syllabus.Ingrid Bergman's character is an alcoholic daughter of a war criminal who's recruited by the US to help spy on the Nazis in South America. Cary Grant plays the agent who recruits her. The two actors had enormous careers, but they've never looked more glamorous and overpowering than in "Notorious."I'm not going to talk the movie to death or go into all the details about the sheer magic Hitchcock created. I will say that one of the most powerful themes that Hitchcock slipped in throughout the film (and most people don't notice) is the relation between alcohol and poison. It's definitely something to watch. All the major dramatic points in the story circle around champagne, wine cellars and poison. Not to say that Hitchcock though alcohol was evil, because I don't think he did. It's just another example of how Hitchcock is able to take simple, everyday things or activities and give them a sinister spin</fullReview>

		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Standard Hitchcock fare which still equates to being well above average.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A solid spy drama with lots of interest for Hitchcock devotee's, 'Notorious' is one of the multitude of films which maintain his reputation without particularly enhancing it.Film combines love story and espionage aspects, as US agent Devlin enlists Alicia Huberman to infiltrate a group of Nazi's based in South America. Grant and Bergman fill these roles well and build up a convincing frosty working relationship, which clearly masks the simmering desire they have for each other. Neither of them move out of 2nd gear though and the film as a whole, isn't that ambitious in it's scope. Claude Rains is the pivotal player on which 'Notorious' could lift itself up a notch, but I found him disappointing in a role he isn't really suitable for. As the sophisticated Nazi agent who falls for Bergman's charms he comes across as too weak and gullible. The storyline puts his character in a difficult position, as it seems ridiculous that he would fall for her and marry so quickly. Apart from being too old for the part ( Bergman was 26 years younger than Rains ), he doesn't have enough menace about him to make the audience feel that Alicia is in any real danger.I don't mean to be harsh on 'Notorious' - the film has some great moments and is solid in all departments. There is a tendency to assume that all Hitchcock film's are works of genius. This is wrong as Hitchcock's reputation rests on the quantity of his work and the high standard maintained over a long period of time. He has a select group of classics, which are then given a rock-solid foundation by a multitude of quality efforts. 'Notorious' is one of these quality efforts.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Truly a Fascinating Piece of Cinema</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I managed to catch this work on AMC's "Murder by Hitchcock." Thank God for AMC on Sunday nights! The story revolves around Alicia Huberman (Portrayed wonderfully by the gorgeous Ingrid Bergman.) who's been recruited by Devlin (Cary Grant.) to infiltrate a clandestine spy ring. You see, her father was a Nazi traitor and it seems the rest of his comrades are still in South America. They're up to something, but I won't tell you what. It doesn't matter much, it's another one of Hitchcock's McGuffins. There are only four real characters worth mentioning. The other two are Sebastian (a magnificient performance by Claude Reins.) and his mother. His mother was played by a Polish stage actress in her only film role. The actress does a wonderful job. The other characters aren't developed enough to leave any impression, but's all for the best. Developing the rest of Sebastian's cohorts and Devlin's allies would have been distracting. Sebastian's cohorts work better as simple "elemental presences", or non-characters who exist only to service plot functions. The three main characters are actually representative of Hitchcock's psyche. Cary Grant was the man Hitchcock wanted to be. Ingrid Bergman was Hitchcock's ideal woman. And Claude Reins stood for Hitchcok's insecurities. The directing in Notorious is top of the line. Catch it.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>among the best of hitchcock</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>this is perhaps my favourite hitchcock film. the edgy chemistry between cary grant and ingrid bergman is not only palpable but also very believable. bergman plays the fast and loose party-girl alicia huberman, the estranged daughter of a german spy. even in her portrayal of a woman with a trashy reputation, bergman is characteristically dignified and elegant, while sometimes showing us the insecure and pathetic aspects of alicia, especially in her relationship with grant's character. and what a bitch grant is in this one! his dark and sexy portrayal of the cynical, emotionally aloof devlin is superb and terribly convincing. claude rains, always a treat to watch, plays alexander sebastian, the nazi friend of alicia's father. alicia is asked by the u.s. government to infiltrate sebastian's home and spy on him and other nazis. meanwhile, a somewhat dark romance develops between alicia and devlin, one in which devlin's often emotionally cruel treatment of alicia (and the fact that she seems to go for it) gives the film an added psychological depth. the film is a masterful integration of romance and espionage thriller. from these two sources hitchcock generates tensions of the highest order since the integrity of neither story is sacrificed for the sake of the other. just as we are held in terrific suspense as to whether alicia will be found out, so we are just as anxious to see how the shaky romance finally pans out. let's just say that hitchcock, again quite masterfully, resolves things on both a heady and a chilling note. splendid flick, highly recommended.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Arguably Alfred Hitchcock's best flick . . .</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>. . . NOTORIOUS! explores the trauma men face when they have to prostitute out chicks on whom they have crushes for America's Greater Good. Just as a rich man never knows for sure whether he's being married just for his dough, a spy master such as T.R. Devlin (Cary Grant) here finds it pretty hard to tell if a girl he's sweet on is willing to sleep with the enemy for love of country or merely love of sexual variety. If such a female can fake coming and going with the bad guy, who's to say that she's not doing the same with any other man, as well? Many experts consider Devlin and his operative Alicia's extended balcony kiss in Rio as the all-time most romantic screen smooch. Yet Hitchcock later said this shot was "inspired" by his observation (from his seat on a train) of a French girl holding onto a guy throughout his outdoors payment of his water bill against a red brick wall in the early 1920s (apparently out-lasting Tom Hank's "record" in front of all those lady ball players in their locker room in A LEAGUE OF OUR OWN). With NOTORIOUS!, Hitch is warning us that females may offer a helping hand one minute, while needing to be smacked unconscious the next (as Devlin does to Alicia during their first car ride together). NOTORIOUS! ends with the now radioactive Alicia representing a ticking time bomb in Devlin's own auto. Hitchcock seems to be preaching, "Guys, don't be taken for a one-way ride!"</fullReview>

		<date>2013-02-19</date>
		<username>hall895</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>A good film but not Hitchcock's best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Notorious is hailed by many as Alfred Hitchcock's best-ever film. And that's quite a stretch. Notorious is a good film. It has a lot of things going for it. But it has some things notably working against it too. There are enough of those elements which don't quite work perfectly to knock this down from the top rung of Hitchcock films. There's much here to enjoy. But there are also things which disappoint.Not your typical Hitchcock suspense thriller Notorious is really more of a romance than anything else. Oh sure there's some mystery and moments of great tension and drama. But at its heart it is really about three people who find themselves entangled in a romantic web. Ingrid Bergman plays Alicia. She's a patriotic American whose father is, rather inconveniently for her, a convicted Nazi spy. She's good at heart but she has a notorious reputation. Known to be a heavy drinker and to sleep around. Toss into the mix her father's crimes and the U.S. government sees an opportunity to use Alicia for their own purposes. This will bring two important men into her life. The first is Devlin, played by Cary Grant. He's the government agent sent to recruit Alicia for her spy mission. And there's Sebastian, the target of the operation. Played by Claude Rains, Sebastian is one of a ring of Nazis who after the war relocated to Brazil. He and his cronies are up to something. The U.S. needs to find out what. Conveniently Sebastian once knew, and was smitten with, Alicia. She's the perfect woman to infiltrate his organization. Sebastian will fall for her instantly. But has Devlin fallen for her too? And who does Alicia really love?Alicia has no trouble winning Sebastian's affection. In fact Sebastian very quickly proposes marriage. By this point it's become pretty clear that Alicia truly loves Devlin. But Devlin won't admit he loves her. He won't admit it to her, he may not even be willing to admit it to himself. Stung by his rejection Alicia goes ahead and marries Sebastian and continues to investigate what exactly is the nefarious activity going on in that house. What the Nazi plot is proves unimportant, a typical Hitchcock MacGuffin. This is a story about three people and their interactions. Sebastian, the supposed villain, is actually a sympathetic character. He truly loves Alicia. She loves another man and if that isn't enough is actually working to destroy Sebastian's life. The tension comes from finding out who will make the crucial discovery first. Will Alicia find Sebastian's secret. Or will Sebastian discover who Alicia really is. If our heroine is found out the consequences for her will obviously be quite dire.It's an interesting setup and a compelling story but it doesn't crackle with the energy of some of Hitchcock's better thrillers. The pacing is very laborious. It's a film in which action is at times hard to come by. The actors do a fine job with it. Bergman is terrific. Rains does an excellent job of making you sympathize with this supposedly villainous man. You feel for Sebastian as the betrayal of the woman he truly loves breaks his heart. Grant comes off as somewhat cold and clipped but that's who his character is. A real standout is Leopoldine Konstantin, who plays Sebastian's mother. Now there's a real villain, this woman is scary. Hitchcock and mothers, you know? These excellent performances elevate the film but not enough to make it truly great. The story, compelling though it may be, ultimately holds the movie back somewhat. It's not consistently entertaining enough, the moments of great drama don't come soon enough or frequently enough. There is a lot to recommend the film. The fine actors, Hitchcock's excellent visual style, and a few really good moments here and there. But the whole package falls just a touch short of being worthy of all the plaudits the film has received. A good film and for Hitchcock an interesting diversion, a romantic thriller that's more about the romance than the thrills. But a film which doesn't quite live up to its reputation.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Very good,...but how did this film rank so high on IMDb?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a very good Alfred Hitchcock film, though it is not one of his more famous productions. Because of this, I was very surprised to see it ranked so high in the IMDb Top 250! Now I am not saying this is a bad film and there are certainly worse films among the exalted few on the list. However, I am pointing out that I feel it is a tad overrated just so viewers don't have expectations that are a bit too high for the film. Of course, it is also possible the film IS that great and I just didn't appreciate it.The film starts with the conviction of a Nazi spy in an American court. His daughter is Ingrid Bergman and in response to all the notoriety from the case, she is leading a life that is sure to kill her sooner or later. What many do NOT realize is that her home has been bugged and our government knows she is not pro-Nazi. So, because her reputation has been ruined by good old dad, she is an excellent candidate to infiltrate a group in Brazil that MIGHT be a group of Nazis hoping to create a new Reich.Unfortunately, the American agent who recruits her is the ever so studly Cary Grant and they naturally fall in love. But, because she needs to infiltrate and even marry someone within this secret group, Grant later responds to Ingrid Bergman with cold indifference--even though it was obvious to everyone they had fallen in love. It's a great example of duty and patriotism superseding everything else.During the infiltration of the Neo-Nazi group, there is a lot of suspense but it is all done in a very restrained way. You know that she is in danger but it is subtle and difficult to detect. Because of these qualities, the film does an excellent job of building tension and involving the viewer.As far as negatives go, the only one (and boy is it minor) is that once again they cast Cary as an American even though he has a lovely English accent. Considering how often this occurred in other films and what a fine actor he was, I was certainly willing to forget this and focus on the excellent movie. The writer (Ben Hecht) and director (Alfred Hitchcock) are both among the more famous in their field and this film is ample evidence of their abilities.By the way, a later effort to re-team of Grant and Bergman and re-kindle the romance on screen was far less successful in INDISCREET--a passable but not especially memorable film.</fullReview>

		<username>JoeytheBrit</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>"Add Sebastien's name to my list of playmates"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Grant and Bergman were both arguably at the height of their success when they made this taut spy thriller for Hitchcock, and both were cast against type as, respectively, a taciturn government agent and a semi-alcoholic tramp. While Grant pulls off his role with skill, Bergman struggles against the enormity of the miscasting of her part even while delivering a first-rate performance. Whoever pictured Bergman for the role of a good-time girl who sleeps around and seems bent on self-destruction clearly wasn't thinking straight. This was the kind of role for which an actress in the mould of Lana Turner might have been the obvious choice, even though she probably wouldn't have brought as much sympathy and sensuousness to the role as Bergman does. Nevertheless, its only when Alicia Sebastien's past is set aside so that the film can concentrate on her tortured relationship with Devlin (Grant) and duplicitous marriage to German ex-pat Alex Sebastien (Claude Rains) that Bergman truly convinces.The Hays Code had some kind of rule about the permitted length of a screen kiss back in the thirties and forties, so Hitchcock got around their puritanical restraints by having Bergman and Grant in a lengthy clinch as they talk to each other on the balcony of her apartment in Rio. Their mouths hardly ever more than millimetres apart, they breath their lines to each other between kisses in an erotically-charged sequence that provides evidence of the ingenuity and inventiveness the Code's restrictions inspired in film-makers of Hitchcock's calibre. Would that scene have existed if the code hadn't existed? Probably not: at least, not in the form that it does, anyway ? and the film would have been weaker for it. It's one of the few intimate scenes the stars share and it's a highlight. After this, their relationship is torn by Alicia's assignment for the government, which is to cosy up to Sebastien and find out what he and his fellow ex-Nazis are up to. The balcony on which they shared their clinch is also the scene of a bitter exchange between the lovers. On the surface they're talking about her mission, but their conversation is really about Alicia sleeping with another man, and this theme of true thoughts and feelings left unspoken runs through the film. When the truth is spoken it is often to devastating effect: "Add Sebastien's name to my list of playmates" Alicia instructs Devlin at the racetrack after she has begun an affair with the Nazi, and the look on Devlin's face says everything you need to know about his true feelings. Grant's character in this film is probably one of the coldest and most reserved that he has played, which leaves the burden of audience identification with Bergman's emotionally damaged Alicia. She shoulders the burden well, despite the miscasting.As with many of Hitchcock's films, the audience is manipulated into identification and sympathy for the villain. We can't help but sympathise with the ultimately pathetic Sebastien (another great performance from Claude Rains, reunited with his Casablanca co-star) as Devlin locks him out of the car in the film's suspenseful finale, in much the same way as we root for Norman Bates in Psycho when the car he tries to submerge in a swamp momentarily stops sinking. No other director I can think of could play with the audience's sympathies in this way. Like Bates, Sebastien is also subjected to the sometimes withering criticism of a demanding mother (Leopoldine Konstantin), and his character's isolation, while not as initially evident as Bates', is every bit as real.There are perhaps fewer of Hitchcock's trademark flourishes in this film, even though it bears all the unmistakable hallmarks of one of his pieces of work. Standout, though, is the slow tracking shot from the top of the stairs to the wine cellar key Bergman grasps in her hand at the party thrown for her by Sebastien. The film begins weakly, and the unconvincing start to Grant and Bergman's love affair is particularly badly handled, but once their relationship is derailed by Alicia's assignment and the internal conflicts come in to play, the film takes off and ratchets up the suspense right to the final shot.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>"Notorious" is far from my favorite Hitchcock film, even though it stars one of my two favorite actors (Cary Grant) and Ingrid Bergman, and supported by one of my favorite character actors -- Claude Rains. However, that is not to say that it is not a good film. It is. So the first question is -- how good is this print and is it worth upgrading to Blu Ray. One of the things I remember well about this film is how dark and indistinct the earlier DVDs were of this film. Unfortunately, the Blu Ray is inconsistent. Sometimes a bit grainy and not crisp, other times better; and this version didn't seem as dark as the older DVDs. Perhaps its just because it was an RKO release, and many of those films did not get the storage care that some of the bigger studios did.You get a bonus in this film -- Cary Grant punching Ingrid Bergman in the face and knocking her cold! As they say -- that alone is worth the price of admission.In the open scenes we learn that Cary Grant is a federal agent, while Ingrid Bergman is the sometimes American patriotic daughter of a convicted Nazi spy...and a heavy drinker and somewhat loose woman. She is convinced to romance a German in Rio whom she used to love (Claude Rains), and she marries him as part of the scheme. But Grant and Bergman are also in love, and there's a lot of sexual tension here.My complaint about this film is not the acting. Grant is great. Bergman is great. Rains is great...even with his bad teeth. I enjoyed seeing Louis Calhern in a supporting role.No, my complaint is that unlike most Hitchcock thrillers, this one isn't thrilling. It plods along. I keep looking at the Blu Ray player to see how much more time until the end. The pace is a very negative factor. And the ending is so abrupt...almost as if they ran out of film stock.If it weren't Grant and Bergman together, this film -- at least for me -- would be a wash. I give it a weak "&amp;".</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Hitchcock's love triangle</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"You could have stopped me with one word, but no, you wouldn't. You threw me at him!"Alfred Hitchcock's 1946 classic romantic spy thriller has tried to be imitated many times by lesser films over the decades, but nearly 70 years later its style and fabulous camera-work continues to leave its mark on audiences everywhere. The master of suspense lived up to his name once again with a slightly different film focusing more on the characters than on the thrills. Set in 1946, with the Second World War recently over, Ben Hecht's screenplay captured the spirit of the era where the fear of a nuclear war breaking out was eminent. American spies were focused on hunting down the remaining German Nazis that had spread throughout the world. The story begins in court where Alicia Guberman's (Ingrid Bergman) father is convicted of being a German spy. Alicia has a reputation of being a heavy drinker and dating many men, and she is ashamed of her father's activities. During one of the parties she hosts at her home she meets Devlin (Cary Grant), an American spy who falls for her beauty and charm, but isn't willing to admit it. He convinces her to travel with him to Rio de Janeiro using her father's connections to spy on his friends. She's asked to approach Alexander Sebastian (Claude Rains), an old family friend who they suspect is also involved with other Nazis in the area. Alicia wins his affection easily, but at the same time it distances her from Devlin who she has feelings for. Once she's in Alexander's home she begins uncovering their secrets, but risking her life at the same time. Notorious relies heavily on the romantic chemistry between Cary Grant and Ingrid Bergman, two of the most talented actors of their era. Honestly, I found the romance between the two of them was rushed, and I didn't buy into it very easily. One has to consider the restrictions however (the Hayes Code for example which restricted kisses to less than three seconds on screen) so there wasn't much more they could do. But that was what made the on and off kiss between the two actors all the more passionate for audiences at the time. Today, however it doesn't have the same effect. I had a difficult time buying into the romance and that affected my enjoyment of the film during the first half. But once the plot settles in Rio de Janeiro, the film has a couple of thrilling scenes. The wine cellar scene was one of the most gripping moments of Notorious and Hitchcock masterfully combined different camera shot and edits to build the suspense accompanying it with Webb's suspenseful score. The final ten minutes also had me at the edge of my seat so the second half of the film made up for the tedious first half. Notorious isn't among my favorite Hitchcock films because he's mastered the suspense in other better movies like Psycho, Vertigo, or Rear Window, while I found his work in Rebecca a more compelling character driven film. Notorious does bank from the strong lead performances from Grant and Bergman, who despite not being able to sell the romantic element at first, they still give engaging performances. Devlin's stubbornness and unwillingness to share his feeling for her, and Alicia's attitude towards his indifference becomes the soul of the film. Claude Rains plays Alexander extremely well, and he gives the strongest performance in the film. He falls in love with Alicia very quickly and that prevents him from seeing what his mother has suspected all along. The influence of Alexander's mother over him is a trademark from Hitchcock and in this film Leopoldina Konstantin plays the mother better than most. It's a fascinating story that slowly builds up to a memorable finale. http://estebueno10.blogspot.com/</fullReview>

		<date>2014-02-25</date>
		<username>Mae Stonelake (MaeStonelake)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Genre, Structure, and soft sexual tension</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Notorious! which was nearly the most expensive of Alfred Hitchcock's films to produce, includes within it's linear narrative, and it's subtle patriotism, a visual style that lends itself to age-old philosophies, where vulnerability and espionage are juxtaposed, while a running theme of love intertwines itself within the suspense. The matters of gender struggle, or more specifically, patriarchal limitations on the female protagonist, are elucidated within the films structure. One facet of the visual style within the formal structure throughout the film is it's literary manufacturing that includes a mechanistic version of Shakespeare's play within a play, only in this sense, as a cinematic story within a story. Some scholars refer to this "side story" as Hitchcock's famous Mcguffin. The motif of secret meeting's centering on Alicia and Devlin, carry with them elements of realism (subtext), full of Hitchcock's dissonant lyricism. To begin with, in 1946 the threat of Nazism was still quite prevalent especially in Alfred Hitchcock's Hollywood where Devlin, played by Cary Grant, is assigned to watch over Alicia Huberman, played by Ingrid Bergman, an independent, previously promiscuous woman, whose father has been convicted to 20 years of custody by the United State's Martial, for spying to assist the Nazis. In the opening scene, where her father is sentenced at the court house, the judge asks the attorneys if there is any reason why this sentence should not take place. Her father interrupts and states, "Yes, I have something to say, You can put me away, but you can't put away what's going to happen to you and to this whole country." This dramatic statement set the tone for the back story that adds to the running theme of anticipated danger. It also gave Alicia's father a tangible character to refer to throughout her plight in the film; she is in all respects, a daughter conflicted by this relationship, which she would otherwise not have as a circumstance for the narrative to play out justifiably. After this dramatic courtroom scene, where men with cameras filled the screen, we transitioned to a party at Alicia's house. Devlin arrived at her party which began with an over-the-shoulder shot of Alicia, exposing only the back of Devlin's head in the frame. This cinematic formula occurs throughout the film, where Devlins back is to the viewer (us) which grants favoritism to Devlin, as the one with our point of view, thus becoming the character we can trust, an element of power, in the visual play as drunken Alicia flirts. The party scene changes into the morning light, where a charming shot through an empty glass bottle opens the scene, textually adding to the visual elements of substances throughout the film. Substances in this film are nearly iconographic. Devlin and Alicia continue their engagement in discussions, drunken struggles, a car sequence, where they are driving and ultimately they are pulled over by a policeman. In the scene in which the two are on the plane, Devlin informs Alicia that her father killed himself in his cell the night before. Alicia seems to be willing to pretend to fall in love with the character Alexander Sebastian following the news, and when suspicious Sebastian has a party, Alicia manages to get the key to the cellar from Sebastian, as Devlin requested which carried us into an important aspect of the cinematic structure of this 1940's thriller. Devlin and Alicia slip off to the wine cellar, where they accidentally drop a bottle, which breaks and black, solidified uranium spills out. Notorious is famous for it's deep crane shot into the cellar in this particular scene. A suspenseful close-up of Alicia's hand with the key to the cellar, (classic Film Noir), as well as a long kiss, one of the longest in film history, and the rotating point of view shot that Alicia see's Devlin in as she suffers a hangover earlier in the film, are all stylistically important to the films incredibly rich appeal to genres. Yet the film's formal aspects of cinematography that relate to the plight of the protagonist are most exquisitely depicted in such a complete sequence within scene 7, the lovely scene with both Devlin and Alicia at the cafe, at the beginning of their time in Rio de Janeiro. The significance of this particular scene, lies in it's transitional element within the film's structure; this scene begins an entirely new chapter in the story of the film. The scene is also very significant because it challenges the dynamics and power struggle between Devlin and Alicia in prior scenes, an otherwise subtle conflict throughout the narrative that serves the questionable nature of woman and the reasonableness of man, ingredients for a true Film Noir. The wonderful dissolves in the cinematography begin while they are on the plane overlooking Rio de Janeiro, yet continuity editing and over-the-shoulder shots tend to Serve the character of Devlin. At moments when Alicia challenges Devlin in the dialogue, she simultaneously reverts back to her love for him, which she crumbles under, extending her role from femme-fatale, to vulnerable. The iconographic images of tilted hats, secret meetings, large groups of men, law enforcement, the motif of substances, the Nazi affiliation of Alexander Sebastian, secret agents and spies, as well as the poison Alicia suffers from, add to the aspects of the film that are conventions for a mystery-thriller sub-genre, and yet the film really does fit within a patchwork genres unlike any of its time!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Yes?yes?notorious.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Alfred Hitchcock directs Notorious with his usual impeccable eye for detail and flair for storytelling, but it is the brilliant pairing of Ingrid Bergman and Cary Grant that really qualifies this film as unmissable viewing, the stars sharing such a smouldering on-screen chemistry that the film's romantic scenes prove to be just as compelling as Hitchcock's trademark moments of suspense (a good job, since the tension isn't ramped up until much later on).A romantic post-war thriller, the film stars Bergman as Alicia Huberman, the wayward party-girl daughter of a Nazi war criminal. With a hatred of her late father's politics, and a love of America, Alicia makes the perfect undercover agent to infiltrate a nest of Nazi scientists up to no good in South America. Accompanying her on her mission: stoic FBI agent Devlin (Grant), who develops more than a professional interest in the lovely lady.As the job becomes more and more dangerous for Alicia, even requiring her to marry old admirer Alexander Sebastian (Claude Rains)?part of the Nazi group?in order to avoid suspicion, Devlin tries his best to ignore his true feelings, but races to the rescue when Sebastian rumbles his new wife and tries to poison her.All of the scenes featuring the beautiful Bergman are guaranteed pleasers as far as I am concerned, but my favourite bit has to be the whole 'key theft/champagne party' segment, which is as good as anything that Hitchcock directed?saying that it is nerve-jangling doesn't do it justice.</fullReview>

		<date>2010-10-02</date>
		<username>Ilpo Hirvonen</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Envy, Politics and the Difficulty of Expression of Emotions</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 1946 Alfred Hitchcock had gained quite a reputation for himself with Rebecca, which won the Best Picture Oscar in 1940, Spellbound, Saboteur and other successful films. His next film was meant to be produced by David O. Selznick, but because of other projects by him and the budget of Notorious, he sold the whole package to RKO which gave the artistic freedom back to Hitchcock. Notorious is a spy-film set in its own post war time. It's a picture of the time just after the WWII and before the Cold War which is quite an interesting period. The film suffered from censorship due to its references to the nuclear bombs of the United States but eventually found its way to the screens.A Nazi's daughter Alicia Huberman (Ingrid Bergman) is being followed by the media and the police because of her father got just an imprisonment. In a party, after the trial, Alicia becomes acquainted with Devlin (Cary Grant) who soon turns out to be a cop. Devlin persuades Alicia to help the Government to get information of his father's Nazi companions who have escaped to Brazil. The leader of the Nazis is Sebastian (Claude Rains) who once was in love with Alicia. The task starts getting unpleasant features when Sebastian falls in love with Alicia again and Alicia falls in love with Devlin, who seems to be neglecting her.Notorious is a very intriguing film. Francois Truffaut called it Hitchcock's greatest love film and the best black-and-white film ever made. And Patricia Hitchcock, daughter of Alfred, has named the film as her personal favorite of her father's films. Notorious has clearly established its place as a Hitchcock-classic. One of the most famous scenes of it is the long kissing scene where Cary and Ingrid kiss each other while talking at the same. This brilliantly built scene full of erotic tension was actually quite sly by Hitchcock. People weren't allowed to kiss longer than 3 seconds in films during the 1940's so Hitchcock decided to give some dialog to the characters in the middle of the kisses and this is actually much more excitatory that just showing a long dull kiss.Many might call Notorious a film-noir whose main hallmarks are the use of shadow and light, moral complexity, the aesthetics of big cities and after WWII; the postwar disillusions. Hitchcock only did a few film-noirs but they were all amazing: Shadow of a Doubt, Spellbound, Suspicion, The Wrong Man and Strangers on a Train. But the strongest portrayal of post-war disillusions is in Notorious -- probably because it was made in the time. The criticism for the politics of the United States is very subtle but today one can see it in the small things; The Government of the United States 'sacrifices' a woman just for its own benefit and the captain Paul Prescott is without a doubt the most obnoxious character of Notorious.The references Hitchcock made about the nuclear bomb made the Government to suspect him and he was actually added to FBI's list of possible foreign agents which Hitchcock himself only found hilarious. Notorious is a brilliant picture of its time but it also works as an entertaining spy-film. No one has been able to build such great excitement as Hitchcock. The narrative and the visual clarity of Notorious are just spellbinding. Notorious is also a clever film about the difficulty of expressing yourself and your emotions. Cary Grant plays this laconic character who clearly is in love with Bergman's character, but somehow can't show it. He becomes jealous of her because of something he forced her to do -- the irony is lovely! Some say that the film is very dark because Hitchcock had just been editing The Memories of the Camps, a documentary about the concentration camps, but I personally think it's just part of the film-noir cinematography which he handled very well. Of course the postwar disillusions made the film what it is but I don't think it would have been any more 'joyful' without The Memories of the Camps. Alfred Hitchcock is the only director who has been able to film directly and without explaining emotions such as suspicion, jealousy, envy and yearning. In Notorious we see a romantic tale in the shadows of espionage and postwar disillusions.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Captivating and truly something to behold.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Master of Suspense, Alfred Hitchcock continued his winning streak through the 1940s with this captivating and truly masterful work that binds together romance and espionage post-World War II. Not only did he have a terrific script by Ben Hecht to work with, he also had two of the greatest stars in Hollywood history at the peak of their powers, Cary Grant as the suave and debonair agent Devlin and Ingrid Bergman as the troubled daughter of a Nazi spy who is asked to infiltrate a group of German engineers in Rio de Janeiro.As it happens, Bergman is trying to forget her troubles of her father being arrested when US agents come calling and ask her to use her background in order to help break a German scientist ring that is cooking up something big in Brazil. She decides to help the agents mostly because she is falling for the contact man, Devlin. Her job, however, calls for her to get involved with the head Nazi, Alex Sebastian, played by the very talented Claude Rains.Something to keep in mind when watching this unfold is that there are essentially two different stories going on at the same time here: the ups and downs of Grant and Bergman's tumultuous relationship and the agents' attempt to break up the German's secret organization. What is also important to note is the fact that when she is assigned to this job, she has fallen for Devlin but now has to mask her feelings for Sebastian. Devlin, in turn, as her contact also has fallen for her but must not show it in order to blow her cover. How Hitchcock balances all these feelings and emotions is incredible and he covers it with an array of shadows, long shots and unbroken takes, including the famous 'longest kiss in movie history' scene.For anyone interested in classic films, this is a must-see. It boasts a great cast, some terrifically twisted moments and one of the best romantic pairings in the cinema. It is also right in the middle of Hitchcock's greatest period in America, when he was becoming comfortable with the system and daring enough to go out on a limb and create stories that were both tantalizing and beautiful.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-01-15</date>
		<username>Kurt Berger</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A pinnacle for the master of suspense.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>For its time, for its place in history, there are precious few films as brazen as Notorious, one of Alfred Hitchcock's most underrated masterpieces. Released in 1946, the film follows a woman of admittedly loose morals, Alicia Huberman (Ingrid Bergman), whose father has just been convicted of treason in aiding Nazi interests during the war. Alicia has developed a reputation, as she's floated from companion to companion, and this latest stigma of a traitorous father combines with her alcoholism to shape a portrait of a truly troubled woman - the kind of which you just did not often see on screen at that time.Into her world comes a man called Devlin, with a subdued portrayal by Cary Grant. Devlin is an American agent who wants Alicia to go to Rio de Janeiro to involve herself with Alex Sebastian (Claude Rains), a war profiteer who is still involved with Nazis who had fled to South America. Alicia, wishing to show that she's changed from her reputation, wants only for Devlin to admit his love for her - a thing he seems unwilling to do. So she forges ahead on her espionage mission, playing at a very dangerous game.All through the brilliant script by Ben Hecht, we see right away the obvious attraction Devlin and Alicia have for each other, but due to their histories and the situation they've involved themselves in, they continually distance themselves from the budding love they could have, and fall into patterns of sharp dialogue and cool emotions that lead them inexorably down a darker path.Alicia's target, Alex, is smitten by this beautiful woman out of his past, even to the chagrin of his domineering mother and his own suspicions towards Devlin. But he himself is a tragic figure, as he's caught in his own webs due to his associates, who brook no deceptions into their plans.All around as the story swirls and complicates, Hitchcock's direction makes thrilling drama out of the simplest things, like the fate of a key at a house party, or involves menace out of a simple cup of coffee. By understating the major players in the film and not over-romanticizing (at least until the end), Hitchcock build suspense without his usual tricks. There's not much deception here - we know what everyone wants; the game is, do they get it? Can they get it? Can these impossible twists turn themselves out right in the end?It's a terrific script, and sharp dialogue by two of cinema's icons can carry a film very far, but the genius of Hitchcock behind the camera transforms it into one of his proudest achievements.</fullReview>
	</review>

It was a slippery slope to walk trying to combine spies and love, but everyone associated with the film walked it with ease. I'm surprised this movie isn't higher on the list, because it is one of Hitchcock's best. 10 out of 10 for creativity, and for the absolutely stellar ending and suspense.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Hitchcock's best love story</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>During his interview of the master, François Truffaut pointed out that Hitchcock was used to shooting murder scenes like love scenes. Notorious stands aside from this usual Hitch's touch. I think this is one of the best love story ever filmed. The characters are caught up in their duties. The entrapment is all the more tight as each one depends on the other. That part of the story can lead to think that opportunist Hitchcock understood the lessons of film noir. 1946 was also the year The Postman always rings twice was adapted.Devlin has to complete his mission because of what is at stake for his country - and for the world, even if he has definitely nothing to do with Bruce Willis. His boss insists on that point and perhaps he chose him to seduce Alicia. Alicia doesn't care much about her father or about national and worldwide interests. She only cares about Devlin. Sebastian doesn't see much more than the love he bears for Alicia. What with his unforgiving mates and his tyrannical mother, his only way to cope with an unfriendly entourage is to love Alicia.Claude Rains himself was entrapted in playing the part of villains entrapted themselves in their nasty jobs (Mr Smith goes to Washington, Casablanca). Sebastian is a real noir hero since he never seems to have the upper hand and loses everything from his villain's authority to love and ...life. Evil is embodied by his disembodied mother who matches Rebecca's Mrs Danvers or later Mrs Bates. Cary Grant never looked less self-confident. His parts in The Awful Truth, Bringing Up Baby and Suspicion ranked him among the ham Hollywood actors that flooded in with the talkies. Here he appears to be more than that. Ingrid Bergman embodies the typical hitchcockian actress, lovely and capable of many things. Joan Fontaine looked too young and innocent for it and Spellbound was not unforgettable - I mean there is more to remember about Dali than about Gregory Peck.I doubt a director will try to remake Notorious today. The interest of the film stands with the joint performance of Cary Grant and Ingrid Bergman, and no technical breakthrough can add anything to that. A Perfect Murder is dull for Michael Douglas was given so poor a script he couldn't bring much to his character. And Gwineth Paltrow will never match Grace Kelly's acting and beauty. I was struck to see how far Dial M for Murder (and The Rope) inspired the creators and writers of the famous TV serial Colombo. Most of the time they reach a better result than the remake. Gus Van Sant's Psycho? Sounds weird. It doesn't deserve further words.</fullReview>

		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Flimsy Espionage</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Despite the awesome personages devoted to this production, one can't overlook the incredible elements of the espionage that it depicts. A spy takes a wine cellar key off her husband's key ring on the night of a party, knowing its immediate value to him, but waits till the next day to slip it back on the ring. She's suspecting he's using the cellar for an enemy military purpose. This same key is his only way to access the wine cellar that he might decide he needs to visit this night. She shouldn't touch this key tonight. If she takes that risk: (a) she's got to be able to return it to his key ring before he goes for more wine; (b) she's also got to be able to return it before he notices for any reason that his keys feel light; (c) if he notices this cellar key missing after seeing her kiss another man right in front of the cellar door, she must realize that he's going to infer that she's discovered his secret; (d) she must be thinking in the following days about how he would respond if he did realize that she's a spy. Since all of this reasoning is basic, this film as it is asks for too much suspension of disbelief: spies would not behave like this. Beyond reasoning, the failure is also in behavior at public meetings of the Bergman and Grant characters. From the first scene, at the racetrack, they try to look like they are not talking to one another, which fails to hide the fact while publicizing the secret nature of their conversation. The Claude Raines character already knew him. He knows he and his wife are friends. Their attempts to stand alongside one another without turning their heads so much while talking makes it obvious that they're talking about something private. This is like a parody of a spy movie, with inept agents fumbling their work. . . . However, the background music is the best.Ingrid Bergman's acting is fine, except for the early, drunk scenes. She seems to be trying to imagine what it feels like to be a little drunk, varying her degrees of drunkenness throughout. For instance, after she realizes her companion is a federal agent, all of her sobriety returns in an instant, her focus, and her anger is fully on the alert. She's an amateur at it.</fullReview>

		<date>2008-01-25</date>
		<username>gscheyd</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>game theory</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I figure to be among the lower raters of this film, but I really rather enjoyed it. On the whole, it has to be considered a success, but broken down into its major components -- a suspense thriller on the one hand, a romantic drama on the other -- a sizable distinction is revealed. As a thriller, it's right up there with Hitchcock's best films; as a romance it's one step up from mediocre (well, maybe a step and a half, but only because we're talking about Ingrid Bergman and Cary Grant here). Also, the romance and thriller elements basically just run in parallel throughout the film, whereas I'd have preferred to see them woven together. My impression was that this never quite happened, though it comes close in a scene or two. (Compare Notorious in these respects to North by Northwest, which I found was superb from both the thriller and romance angles and involved more influence of each on the other.) The film's greatest achievement lies in its presentation of the precarious balance created as characters begin uncovering each other's secrets. The espionage makes for a wonderfully complicated bit of game theory. There are certain facts known to all of the characters, others that are secrets held only by one or two characters, others that were secrets but have since been discovered by another character (unbeknownst to those who formerly held the secrets), and still other former secrets whose discovery has been discovered by those who originally held them (unbeknownst to the discoverer who now overestimates his or her advantage). If it seems a bit overdone, it's only because I couldn't think of a better way of concisely explaining it. The film is in fact remarkably elegant in fitting these pieces together.Had the project been handed to a lesser director, I'm sure it all would have come across as stilted and forced; either that or a lot of the material would have just been cut to make the story more manageable. It's the sort of story one might read in a novel and think, "This would make such a great movie, but they really couldn't do it justice in under four hours. Oh well." This one weighs in at around 100 minutes I think. It's quite an accomplishment in that respect, and if the love story falls short of Casablanca, well I can forgive it for that.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Wherein lies the notoriety?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The scene at the end - the one involving the staircase - is terrific, and I don't know Hitchcock did it. This isn't a technical remark. What I mean is it seems as if there's nothing to it, as if nothing is even happening, and yet it's remarkably tense; in fact, one of the great moments in cinema. If you watch it and succumb easily to short-term amnesia you might well endorse the common verdict (given by François Truffaut, among others) that `Notorious' is Hitchcock's masterpiece.The trouble is that the story is for the most part remarkably uninvolving. Ingrid Bergman plays the role of a Fallen Woman - a character type that never fails to irritate, and which I would have thought was hopelessly out of date in 1946 - although, given the shameful way Bergman herself was hounded out of America few years later, it seems that people still believed in Fallen Women. Well: surely we don't believe in them NOW. As a result it's hard to take the bulk of the story seriously. Why, exactly, is Cary Grant so hard on Bergman for so long? No reason that I can see. The love story is merely exasperating. The MacGuffin is actually more interesting than the MacGuffee.`Spellbound', `Lifeboat', `Rope', and several other of Hitchcock's films of the 1940s, may seem more dated to some, but that's to take a superficial view. The passage of time has revealed that there was nothing wrong with their basic stories. Not so `Notorious'.</fullReview>

		<date>2005-11-18</date>
		<username>James Hitchcock</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Ostensibly a thriller, but deserves to be remembered as a psychological drama</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The film starts with John Hubermann, an American citizen of German origin, being sentenced to imprisonment for treason after being convicted of spying for the Nazis. The main character, however, is not Hubermann himself but rather his daughter Alicia who is recruited by American Intelligence to infiltrate a spy ring of her father's former associates. Alicia travels to Rio de Janeiro, where the gang are based, accompanied by Devlin, a handsome intelligence agent. Once there she meets Alex Sebastian, a friend of her father and an old admirer of hers, who is a member of the gang. Sebastian is still very much in love with Alicia, and she agrees to marry him in order to gain access to the gang and discover their secrets.1946 was perhaps not the best year in which to set a film with an espionage theme. The Second World War was over, but relations between the West and the Soviet Union had not yet deteriorated to the extent where they could be considered a cold war. The plot might have worked better if Hitchcock, had set it a few years earlier, during the war itself, but he was obviously unwilling to do this. Perhaps he felt that a film set in the present would have more impact than one set in the past, even the very recent past. Seen as a spy thriller, in fact the film is not a very good one, certainly not when compared to, say, "The 39 Steps" or "North by North-West", and the plot is rather carelessly put together. We never find out exactly what the Nazi gang are plotting, although as uranium is involved it presumably concerns some attempt to put together a nuclear device. What makes the film interesting, however, is the high standards of acting from the three leads, Cary Grant as Devlin, Ingrid Bergman as Alicia and Claude Rains, as Sebastian, and the relationships between the characters they play.In a number of Hitchcock's films there is an element of moral ambiguity about some of the characters, both heroes and villains. In "Strangers on a Train", for example, the villain, Bruno, is in a sense the alter ego of the hero, Guy. The murder at the centre of the film, of Guy's ex-wife Miriam, is carried out by Bruno, but there is a suggestion that Guy suffers from feelings of guilt both about her death and about the failure of their marriage. Shortly before she died they had a very public quarrel in which he shouted that he could strangle her. In "Rebecca" Maxim is presented as a sympathetic character, but he bears some of the responsibility for the death of his first wife (although not as much as he did in Daphne du Maurier's original novel).There is a similar ambiguity in "Notorious". On a political level, Sebastian is a Nazi and therefore by definition one of the bad guys. Devlin and Alicia are trying to expose him in the interests of American democracy and are therefore by definition good guys. If, however, we simply view the characters on a human level without ideological preconceptions, things look very different. Sebastian is an older man deeply in love with his beautiful young wife, but is unaware that she has married him not out of love but in order to use him for her own purposes. (Sebastian is, like a number of other Hitchcock villains such as Bruno or Norman Bates in "Psycho" a Freudian case-study; the most powerful influence in his life is his dominating elderly mother. The atmosphere inside his house is one of oppressive and claustrophobic opulence, similar to the effect Hitchcock achieved with Manderley in "Rebecca".) Moreover, Alicia's partner in her deception of her husband, Devlin, is the man with whom she is really in love. Although Alicia pretends to be in love with Sebastian, she is in reality quite indifferent to him and has no qualms about abandoning him to his fate at the hands of his associates, who punish ruthlessly those they believe to have failed them. Whatever we feel about Sebastian, we cannot help feeling a chill when, in the final shot of the film, the door slams shut behind him.As with Guy, there is a suggestion that Alicia may be motivated by guilt, in her case guilt about her father's treason. Although she despised his activities and took no part in them, she nevertheless knew about them and failed to report him to the authorities. The Alicia that we see early in the film is an alcoholic (a change from Ingrid Bergman's normally pure and wholesome image) and the implication is that her drinking is the result of her guilt feelings. Images of alcohol, in fact, recur throughout the film, with numerous shots of people drinking or with bottles and glasses very prominent. The secret to the gang's activities is eventually found inside a wine bottle.As normal with Hitchcock, there are some effective moments of suspense, especially the scene where Devlin and Alicia are searching Sebastian's wine cellar, and the one at the end where Devlin has to try to rescue Alicia from Sebastian's house under the noses of the Nazi gang. Nevertheless this is a film that, although ostensibly a thriller, deserves to be remembered as a first-class psychological drama. 8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Hardly Hitchcock...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The main problem about this movie is that it's more of a romance movie than a horror/thriller movie, which results in it being very boring and predictable. I have no idea why it is so popular; it doesn't even come close to being as great as Hitchcock's later great movies, like Psycho &amp; Vertigo. In fact, if it wasn't for a few sparse camera angles and cinematic effects scattered throughout the movie, I wouldn't have believed it was even made by Hitchcock. The plot is ok, but it doesn't start off until half-way into the movie, which makes everything up to that point seem boring and unnecessary, which it pretty much is. The acting is good, but good acting can't save such a boring, slow-moving plot. The romance part of the movie takes up way too much room; I know that the romance parts in this wasn't cliches until years later, but I still think they're far too boring to sit through. The entire movie is mostly boring, apart from the last 20 minutes, which contain a pretty decent amount of classic Hitchcock suspense, but unfortunately it's too little, too late. As such, it can't save the movie from being boring, drawn-out and more or less pointless. I guess, considering the subject matter, it had an impact when it was made in '46, but by now, it has no power as a political drama, and we're left with a cliche-ridden romance with little to no excitement or suspense. If you like Hitchcock, go watch Psycho or Vertigo instead. They're far better than this. I'd only recommend this to people who want to see how Hitchcock started out, as there are several very typical Hitchcockian ideas in this; unfortunately, they're severely underused, and the film is left being dull and filled to the limit with romantic sap, rather than actual excitement. 4/10</fullReview>

		<username>bandw</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Shows its age, but worth seeing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's hard to go too far wrong when you have Cary Grant and Ingrid Bergman playing off each other with Hitchcock directing. Bergman is asked by a U.S. intelligence agent (Grant) to infiltrate a group of Nazis who have regrouped in Rio after the war to continue plotting nefarious deeds. Of course Bergman and her primary contact fall in love. However, it's a complicated situation since, in order to preserve her cover, Bergman is forced to marry one of the Nazis whom she had known in the past. The romance between Bergman and Grant is necessarily restrained by circumstance and Grant plays his part perhaps too well, since you hardly perceive his passion. Bergman has a difficult time playing against type as a drunken party girl in the initial scenes, but she recovers herself for the rest of the film. There is some interesting camera work and lighting. Grant's predicament raises the issue of how much of a personal life an intelligence agent is willing to give up in favor of duty to country and job. This theme is examined more sharply in movies like the more recent, "The Good Shepard," and the brilliant "The Spy Who Came in From the Cold.""Notorious" is dated in a lot of ways. Heavy use of soft focus close-ups has gone out of style. I am sure Mothers Against Drunk Driving would be made apoplectic by Bergman's being totally bombed and taking off driving on country roads at high speed. Of course she fortunately did not encounter a single other car in that escapade. And smoking has ceased being cool. Some little things bothered me, like the fact that people were sleeping under heavy blankets in Rio in mid-spring. The thing that really got to me was the heavy use of obviously fake backgrounds: Rio beach scenes from a hotel balcony, backgrounds at outdoor cafés, scenes from car windows, and so forth. This distanced me from the movie by making me realize I was watching actors on a set. The tension builds as things progress. Has a handsome prince ever saved a damsel in distress in a more cleverly plotted final scene?</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A little disappointment...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Psycho, Vertigo, North by Northwest and the others. Who can interrogate the quality of these films because of the ability of The Master? 		<shortDescription>Saved by Rains</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I first set eyes on the cover of the videotape, I'm afraid my reaction was "Cary Grant? I don't remember Cary Grant's being in this!" I'd retained the memory of Claude Rains and Ingrid Bergman, in that order -- and as the film wound on, it became all too obvious why I'd apparently forgotten Grant altogether. His character is a wooden nonentity.As a romance, I found this sorely lacking; I quite literally had difficulty keeping awake during the first half. Devlin is thoroughly unlikable and Alicia is awash in noxious self-pity (not to mention stronger liquids). The chemistry between them failed to convince: I really can't read Devlin as the repressed, impassioned type lashing out through inner torment as has been claimed. This is no embittered, self-hating cynic, let alone a 'sexy' one -- just a rather unconvincing spy. I have a problem with films where the only person I actually care about is the villain, but it wasn't until Claude Rains turned up that the film became interesting to me at all -- and certainly by the end I caught myself willing him to succeed in getting rid of the unloving wife who has betrayed him so badly.As a thriller it succeeds better; that and Rains' performance are the only reason I scored it as high as I have. But the actual tense section is really confined to the big set-piece party scene (where our hero and heroine are as spectacularly incompetent at not appearing suspicious as ever). The only person I'm afraid for in the ending is, again, Rains -- the romantic reconciliation doesn't warm my heart (since I couldn't give tuppence for either of the participants by this point) and of course the patriotic message leaves me completely cold, as a US outsider.A brittle, bravura performance by Ingrid Bergman; Grant is very much in the background. Rains steals the film, and/or saves it. Either way, I'm not sure I very much *like* it...</fullReview>

		<username>trimmerb1234</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Radioactive Soap: only the Stars make it shine.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie had most of the very the best Hollywood, then or now, could offer lavished on it. The biggest stars, one of its greatest directors. And one of its great scriptwriters in Ben Hecht too. What then turned what could have been a great movie into this middling and rather brainless effort? How, given the magical nature of all the ingredients, could this have happened? Other reviewers berate the director, Hitchcock, but directors only tell the story, they don't write it. How did such a poor script get put on celluloid? It did though serve one of its stars well enough - Ingrid Bergman was scarcely ever more lovely or, as a loving, abused and endangered heroine, ever tugged so at male audiences' heart-strings (exactly as she does the Cary Grant character) other than perhaps as she had done in "Gaslight". The casting otherwise did seem less than sure-footed. Cary Grant's line of dry humour under adversity is entirely lacking and he is less convincing as the police agent torn between love and duty - the less-than-sharp and rather misogynistic script unhelpful. Claude Rains was simply too nice to be a Nazi - and too old to be the mother-dominated son of a woman played by an actress in reality just 3 years his senior.Taken as a thriller it suffers from various defects. Filmed biographies of real life figures such as "Odette" (1950) have left little creditable room for soapy variations on the theme of the woman spy facing extreme danger and death. Common sense insists that such heroines' motivations required to be of the very strongest kinds in order to undertake such dangerous and distasteful work yet here plausible motivation is entirely lacking. And the agents who set her up seem to take her extreme commitment for granted. The plot was decidedly leaky - Grant and Bergman meet publicly, sit and embrace on a park bench in the city where she lives a lie with her Nazi husband as if there was no danger of being watched. And finally, the coup de grace for a thriller - it distinctly lacked tension.Take away the top line stars and director and this would have been a pot-boiling "B" movie. Indeed take away Ingrid Bergman and not that much worth watching would have been left.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Not typical Hitchcock - so I like it!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Notorious" (1946): Directed by Alfred Hitchcock, starring Cary Grant, and Ingrid Bergman. I am NOT a fan of Hitchcock, and this is NOT like a typical Hitchcock film, so I liked it much better. It's without all the last minute twist gimmicks and typical A.H. signature devices so many people seem to appreciate. This is a straight ahead crime-suspense-drama, set in current time (1946), at the end of WWII, reflecting real concerns and events. In THIS case it means hunting down Nazis who escaped to South America. Grant is a government Agent, Bergman is a somewhat unwilling part-time Agent ? the daughter of a treasonous father. Together, they are assigned the infiltration of a group of Germans down in Rio. This is strong, steamy, tense, reality based stuff, with, as you might expect, some sort of push/pull between our two leads? and WHO could resist Bergman (or Grant)? There are very few cheezie special effects (such as in "Vertigo" ? one I especially dislike), and forget trying to spot Hitchcock hiding somewhere ? he's not there. Maybe he respected this story or his actors enough to avoid such distractions?</fullReview>

		<fullReview>'Notorious (1946)' is one of Hitchcock's beloved mysteries, but watching it now, restored on Criterion Collection DVD, it seems just a bit trivial. Cary Grant is a mostly serious government agent, not his usual rascal self. Ingrid Bergman is superb as the daughter of a convicted German spy. Most of the movie takes place in Rio, where Alicia (Bergman) is to help the US government find out why a group of expatriate Germans are there and what their scheme is. Devlin (Grant) is an agent for the Brazilian government and his job is to be Alicia's contact there. The movie was actually shot in a Hollywood studio, and a 2nd unit filmed generic scenes in and around Rio which were used via rear projection to provide backgrounds for the actors. This is explained in one of the features in the DVD's 'dossier.'SPOILERS follow - Alicia is a woman of loose morals, and perfect for the assignment to get 'close' to the Germans in Rio, but Devlin falls in love with her. Duty prevents him from revealing his true feelings, instead he encourages her to accept the German's marriage proposal. Hitchcock's 'mcguffin' is the sandy material Devlin and Alicia find in a wine bottle while snooping in the cellar during a party. The sand turns out to be a source of Uranium, but that doesn't matter to this movie. When her German husband and his mum find out she knows about the cellar, they begin to slowly poison her, because if they revealed the discovery to their cronies, they would be disposed of. The climax comes when Devlin, after not hearing from Alicia in a week, visits their home, boldly goes to her bedroom, finds the truth, and walks Alicia out and down the stairs, the husband knowing he cannot reveal the truth or his time is up too. The movie ends with the large door closing with a great 'THUD' on the Germans, and we know what will happen. Meanwhile ... Alicia and Devlin start their life together.A rather simple story, well done in black and white, but comes across as quite dated in this 21st century.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Notoriously overrated</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Notorious" is often hailed as one of Alfred Hitchcock's finest productions, and with a cast that includes Cary Grant, Ingrid Bergman, and Claude Rains, why shouldn't it be? Well, lets see if I can think of a reason. In addition to having oily fingertips, my head scratching has produced several reasons why "Notorious" does not deserve its superior reputation: first of all, it's dull. If that were not enough to sink it, and I believe it is, it's also boring. Dull and boring: the cardinal sins of filmmaking, sins that Hitchcock had previously committed with "Rebecca," and would commit again with "The Paradine Case," "Stage Fright," and "Under Capricorn." The "master of suspense" did not earn that title through those films, and there is little suspense to be found in this supposed "espionage thriller" that is, in fact, a romance.Cary Grant and Ingrid Bergman smooch up a storm while resident villain Claude Rains steals the acting honors as a Nazi spy. The whole tedious affair reminds me that of the four Hitchcock films starring Grant, only 1959's "North by Northwest" qualifies as a classic. The others--"Suspicion," "To Catch a Thief," and, yes, "Notorious"--are forgettable.</fullReview>
	</review>
